                                            A Communication-Theoretic Framework for LLM
                                                Agents: Cost-Aware Adaptive Reliability


                                                                          Hamed Omidvar, Vahideh Akhlaghi
                                                                             INTELLERCE LLC
                                                                              San Jose, CA, USA
                                                             {hamed.omidvar, vahideh.akhlaghi}@intellerce.com




arXiv:2605.09121v1 [cs.LG] 9 May 2026
                                                                                        Abstract
                                                    Agents built on large language models (LLMs) rely on a range of reliability
                                                    techniques, including retry, majority voting, and self-consistency, that have been
                                                    developed in parallel rather than within a common analytical framework. We
                                                    observe that an LLM sampled at temperature T is a discrete stochastic channel
                                                    p(y | x) in the sense of Shannon’s coding theory, and use this identity as the entry
                                                    point for such a framework grounded in communication theory. Each of these
                                                    techniques is a special case of one of six classical reliability operators: diversity
                                                    combining, hybrid retransmission, iterative generator-critic decoding, rateless
                                                    sampling, structured redundant verification, and difficulty-adaptive routing. Within
                                                    the framework we give two closed-form results: a noise-variance threshold above
                                                    which uniform averaging beats quality-weighted averaging, and a contractivity
                                                    criterion for generator-critic refinement, consistent with a contractive-to-divergent
                                                    transition we observe between 3B- and 14B-parameter models. We further
                                                    introduce a cost-aware semantic-nearest-neighbor router whose single Lagrangian
                                                    knob traverses the quality-cost frontier without retraining. Across six channel
                                                    configurations spanning local and cloud models on 69 hard tasks, no fixed model-
                                                    technique-budget choice dominates, motivating per-task allocation. On a 300-item
                                                    hard split of MMLU, GSM8K, and HumanEval, our router occupies the full
                                                    empirical Pareto frontier: at matched quality, its normalized cost is ≈56% lower
                                                    than the strongest fixed technique; at matched normalized cost, it improves quality
                                                    by ≈7% (26% over single-shot decoding). These results argue for consolidating
                                                    these reliability techniques into a single tunable layer informed by channel coding.


                                        1    Introduction
                                        Large language models (LLMs) have become the computational backbone of autonomous AI agents,
                                        powering applications from code generation to scientific reasoning [OpenAI, 2023, Anthropic, 2024].
                                        Yet a fundamental tension persists: individual LLM calls are stochastic and unreliable. Output
                                        quality varies substantially with prompt formulation, sampling temperature, and model choice.
                                        Hallucination, logical gaps, and omissions occur unpredictably, particularly on tasks near the frontier
                                        of a model’s capability. Current approaches to agent reliability—retry loops, majority voting, and
                                        ensemble methods—each address specific failure modes but lack a unified theoretical framework that
                                        would provide principled design criteria across techniques. Communication engineering offers such a
                                        framework: from Shannon’s channel capacity theorem [Shannon, 1948] through turbo [Berrou et al.,
                                        1993] and low-density parity-check (LDPC) codes [Gallager, 1962], it has developed a principled
                                        toolkit for extracting reliable information from unreliable channels.
                                        Core insight. An autoregressive LLM with weights θ, conditioned on a fixed system prompt with
                                        any in-context examples and sampled at temperature T , defines a conditional distribution pθ (y | x)
                                        over outputs y given task input x. The weights θ and the system context are held fixed across calls
                                        and together specify the channel; T is a tunable channel parameter (suppressed in the notation for
                                        Preprint.
brevity). Treating each complete generation as a single channel use, pθ (y | x) satisfies the definition
of a channel transition probability W (y | x) [Shannon, 1948, Cover and Thomas, 2006]. Conditional
on this channel context, temperature controls the sampling component of noise, while model capacity,
training-data coverage, and task-distribution mismatch jointly determine an irreducible “floor” noise
that persists even at T = 0; token-level log-probabilities provide intrinsic soft-decision channel output.
Mutual information, channel capacity, diversity combining, and coding gain all have well-defined
analogs in this setting, yielding both technique transfer and quantitative predictions about failure
modes and operating regimes.
Contributions.
1. Agent channel model and a unified framework with explicit prior-art mapping. We formalize
   the autoregressive language model as a discrete stochastic channel (Sec. 3) and introduce
   AGENT C ODEC (Sec. 4), which instantiates six communication-theoretic reliability operators
   adapted for LLM agents: diversity combining (selection, equal-gain, maximal-ratio), hybrid
   retransmission (chase combining and incremental redundancy), iterative generator-critic decoding,
   rateless sampling, structured redundant verification, and difficulty-adaptive routing. Each widely
   cited inference-time reliability method (Self-Consistency [Wang et al., 2023], Best-of-N [Chow
   et al., 2025], Confidence-Informed Self-Consistency [Taubenfeld et al., 2025], Mixture-of-
   Agents [Wang et al., 2025], Self-Refine [Madaan et al., 2023], Multi-Agent Debate [Du et al.,
   2024], Chain-of-Verification [Dhuliawala et al., 2024], FrugalGPT/RouteLLM [Chen et al.,
   2024, Ong et al., 2025]) is a special case of one of these operators at a specific hyperparameter
   setting (Tables 2 and 6); each AGENT C ODEC variant adds one mechanism that the corresponding
   prior method omits, so at matched call budget it is expected to weakly dominate its baseline,
   empirically confirmed on 6 of 7 head-to-head comparisons (Table 5), with the one exception itself
   predicted by the framework (Sec. 5.2).
2. Two analytical results tying agent behavior to classical decoding theory (Sec. 3.4). We
   characterize a channel-state-information (CSI) noise crossover (Prop. 1) that sharpens classical
   noisy-CSI results on maximal-ratio combining [Tomiuk et al., 1999, Gao et al., 2003, Simon
                                                                                       ∗2
   and Alouini, 2005] into a one-line diagnostic with closed-form critical variance σw    above which
   equal-gain combining dominates noisy maximal-ratio combining, reading the local-versus-cloud
   reversal of Sec. 5.3 as crossing this threshold. We also give a fixed-point characterization of
   the iterative-decoding threshold (Prop. 2) that identifies the LLM refinement operator with
   a one-dimensional extrinsic-information-transfer (EXIT) recursion whose contraction-versus-
   expansion dichotomy |f ′ (q ∞ )| ≶ 1 is consistent with the 3B/8B/14B transition we observe; the
   dynamical-systems content is classical, our contributions are the modeling identification and the
   explicit role of the best-of-sequence guard.
3. A cost-aware semantic-nearest-neighbor router. We introduce a dispatcher that embeds each
   task with a sentence encoder, looks up the per-technique mean quality and cost over its k nearest
   training-cache neighbors, and selects the technique that maximizes a single cost-regularized
   objective with one Lagrangian knob λ (Sec. 5.4, Eq. (3)). The same router instance traverses
   the empirical quality-cost frontier without retraining: on 300 hard-benchmark items it beats the
   cross-validated best-fixed-technique policy by ≈6.8% in mean quality at matched cost, and at
   matched quality runs at ≈55.7% lower normalized cost than fixed-best.
4. Controlled empirical evaluation across six channel configurations. We report paired-per-task
   quality and cost on 69 curated tasks (four categories) and on a 300-task hard split of MMLU,
   GSM8K, and HumanEval, across six configurations: three local Ollama scales (3B/8B/14B),
   an Anthropic + OpenAI cloud pair (Claude Haiku 4.5 + GPT-5-mini), and an Ollama-cloud trio
   (Nemotron-Nano-3 30B + Devstral-Small-2 24B channels, GLM-5.1 judge). The runs empirically
   trace the iterative-decoding threshold of Prop. 2 across model scales (Fig. 3).

2   Related work
We review the communication-theoretic foundations in Sec. A and focus here on positioning our
work. The core communication-theory primitives we use are: selection combining (SC, picking
the best of several copies), equal-gain combining (EGC, averaging copies with equal weight),
maximal-ratio combining (MRC, weighting copies by their channel-state-information / SC quality
estimate), hybrid automatic repeat request (HARQ, with the chase-combining variant HARQ-CC
and the incremental-redundancy variant HARQ-IR), forward error correction (FEC), and adaptive
coding and modulation (ACM, selecting among modulation-and-coding schemes (MCS) according
to a channel-quality indicator).


                                                    2
Sampling and voting. Self-consistency [Wang et al., 2023], its confidence-informed exten-
sions [Taubenfeld et al., 2025, Feng et al., 2025, Cordero-Encinar and Duncan, 2025], best-of-
N [Chow et al., 2025, Lightman et al., 2024], and verifier-weighted best-of-N [Li et al., 2023] are
selection combining (SC), with the weighted variants amounting to MRC over the discrete answer
space. Test-time compute scaling [Snell et al., 2025, Roberts et al., 2026, Zhu et al., 2025] identifies
parallel and sequential regimes mapping to diversity combining and HARQ / turbo coding. Ensem-
bles and routing. Mixture-of-Agents (MoA) [Wang et al., 2025] is equal-gain combining (EGC);
Self-MoA [Li et al., 2025] is time diversity + EGC; Attention-MoA [Wen et al., 2026] approaches
maximal-ratio combining (MRC). LLM-Blender [Jiang et al., 2023] performs rank-then-fuse [Chen
et al., 2025]. FrugalGPT [Chen et al., 2024] and RouteLLM [Ong et al., 2025] are degenerate adaptive
coding and modulation (ACM), adapting only the model without redundancy control. Iterative refine-
ment. Self-Refine [Madaan et al., 2023] and Reflexion [Shinn et al., 2023] resemble hybrid automatic
repeat request with incremental redundancy (HARQ-IR) without an extrinsic-information constraint.
Multi-agent debate [Du et al., 2024, Ning et al., 2025] corresponds to turbo decoding but lacks the
extrinsic-only exchange that is central to classical turbo decoders. LLM-as-a-Judge [Zheng et al.,
2023] is a noisy channel estimator [Gu et al., 2026, Schroeder and Wood-Doughty, 2024]. Each prior
method can be understood as an instance of one communication-theoretic technique; our framework
makes this mapping explicit and evaluates all six within a single experimental setup (Tables 1 and 2).

3     The agent channel model
3.1    Agent as stochastic channel
Definition 1 (Agent Channel). Fix a finite token vocabulary V and let V ∗ denote the set of all
finite-length token sequences over V. Let the task (prompt) space be T ⊆ V ∗ and the output space
Y = V ∗ . An autoregressive language model with parameters θ defines, for each input x ∈ T and
each sequence y = (y1 , . . . , yL ) ∈ Y of length L = |y|, the conditional probability
                                                          |y|
                                                          Y
                                        pθ (y | x) =            pθ (yt | y<t , x),                                     (1)
                                                          t=1

where t ∈ {1, . . . , |y|} indexes token position, yt ∈ V is the token at position t, y<t := (y1 , . . . , yt−1 )
denotes the prefix preceding position t (with y<1 := ∅, the empty sequence, and an end-of-sequence
symbol terminating the product), and pθ (yt | y<t , x) is the next-token distribution produced by the
model’s softmax output. We call Aθ : T → Y, Y ∼ pθ (· | x), an agent channel; it is a discrete
stochastic channel that is memoryless across independent invocations, each complete generation
constituting a single channel use1 . The conditional pθ (y | x) satisfies the axioms of a channel
transition probability W (y | x) [Shannon, 1948, Cover and Thomas, 2006]: it is non-negative for
every (x, y) and sums to one over y ∈ Y for every fixed x. The channel’s noise characteristics
(temperature-controlled per-token entropy, model-dependent quality) are structured enough for
communication-theoretic reliability techniques to transfer, as we demonstrate.
Remark 1 (Temperature as the noise parameter). Let T > 0 denote the sampling temperature.
Temperature sampling rescales the per-token logits and renormalizes, replacing pθ (· P   | y<t , x) with
pT (v | y<t , x) ∝ pθ (v | y<t , x)1/T for v ∈ V. Writing HT (Yt | y<t , x) := − v∈V pT (v |
y<t , x) log pT (v | y<t , x) for the per-token Shannon entropy under pT , one has HT → 0 as T → 0+
(deterministic channel) and HT → log |V| as T → ∞ (uniform-noise channel). Temperature
therefore continuously interpolates between a deterministic and a pure-noise channel, controlling the
sampling component of channel noise. However, even at T → 0 (greedy decoding), the channel is
not noiseless: errors from limited model capacity, training-data gaps, and task-distribution mismatch
persist as an irreducible “floor” noise, analogous to residual interference in communications.
Definition 2 (Quality Function and Channel Estimation). A quality function is any map q : T × Y →
[0, 1] that takes a (task, output) pair (x, y) to a scalar quality score, with q = 1 a perfect answer and
q = 0 a wholly wrong one. The agent channel itself supplies an intrinsic, judge-free reliability mea-
    1 The autoregressive factorization in (1) introduces memory within a sequence; treating each (prompt, response) pair as

one channel use restores memorylessness across uses, the standard block-coding assumption [Cover and Thomas, 2006].
This applies to independent invocations only: multi-turn chains that condition on prior turns (HARQ and turbo, which feed
critic feedback into the generator’s prompt) violate it and are analyzed as channels with feedback in Secs. 5.3 and M; the
memoryless analysis is used here only for single-turn parallel techniques (diversity combining, fountain, FEC).


                                                            3
sure: given a realized output y ∼ pθ (· | x) of length L = |y|, the per-token negative log-likelihood is
                                                     |y|
                                                 1 X
                            H(Aθ , x, y) = −            log pθ (yt | y<t , x),                        (2)
                                                |y| t=1

where t and pθ (yt | y<t , x) are as defined above. Equation (2) is the response-level analogue of the
channel’s average per-token Shannon entropy and is the standard agent-uncertainty proxy used in the
calibration literature [Kadavath et al., 2022, Kuhn et al., 2023]. Except for variants discussed later
that require the availability of log-probabilities, we implement q as a separate large language model
(the “judge”), an external channel estimator that plays the role of pilot-based signal-to-noise-ratio
estimation in classical receivers; this introduces estimator noise that we discuss in Remark 3.
3.2 Channel Quality and Operating Point
Let pX  be the task distribution over     T . The expected channel noise rate is H̄(Aθ , T ) :=
EX∼pX EY ∼pT (·|X) [H(Aθ , X, Y )] , the average over both the task distribution and the sampling
distribution at temperature T , and depends on model parameters θ (neural scaling laws show that
training cross-entropy loss decays as L ∝ N −α in the model parameter count N [Kaplan et al., 2020,
Hoffmann et al., 2022], which upper-bounds H̄ on in-distribution tasks), temperature T (Remark 1),
and task difficulty [Yadkori et al., 2024]. Model capacity and task difficulty are dual: a hard task
on a 70B model may produce the same H̄ as an easy task on a 3B model, so ACM routing by
difficulty (Sec. 4) is equivalent to adapting the coding scheme to the operating point [Goldsmith and
Chua, 1997]. This duality predicts that a given technique transitions from harmful to beneficial as H̄
decreases, crossing a decoding threshold [ten Brink, 2001]; we confirm this for HARQ in Sec. 6. Full
derivations appear in Sec. M.
3.3 Diversity Order
Definition 3 (Agent Diversity Order). Given d ≥ 1 agent channels A1 , . . . , Ad , each independently
generating Yi ∼ pθi (· | X) with quality scores qi := q(X, Yi ) ∈ [0, 1], the ensemble has diversity
                       Q τ ∈ (0, 1), if the qi are independent the ensemble outage satisfies
order d. For a threshold
P(maxi qi < τ ) =        i Pout,i (τ ) with Pout,i (τ ) := P(qi < τ ), decaying geometrically in d.
Correlation through shared training data or aligned prompts gives an effective diversity deff ≤ d
(Remark 3; empirically deff ∈ [1.14, 1.71] across our configurations, Table 12 and Sec. M).
Remark 2 (MRC weighting heuristic). By analogy with SNR-proportional MRC in           Padditive white
Gaussian noise (AWGN) channels [Proakis and Salehi, 2008], we set wiMRC = qi / j qj . Because
our combiner is a nonlinear LLM and qi a noisy judge estimate, we treat this as a heuristic and rely on
a cyclic-redundancy-check (CRC) style regression guard (Sec. 4) to ensure no degradation versus SC.
The CSI-noise crossover that quantifies when this heuristic loses to EGC is in Remark 3 and Sec. 3.4.
Remark 3 (CSI quality and the MRC–EGC reversal). Remark 2 requires branch independence and
accurate CSI. In practice LLM outputs are correlated (deff ≤ d) and numeric judge scores are a noisy
estimate; we partially mitigate the latter with a sigma-delta-style binary checklist judge (Sec. 4).
Residual imperfect-CSI effects can still attenuate MRC’s gain over EGC [Gao et al., 2003, Simon
                                                          ∗2
and Alouini, 2005], quantified by the critical variance σw   of Prop. 1. Token log-probabilities offer a
complementary intrinsic soft-decision channel that realizes classical soft-input soft-output (SISO)
MRC [Kadavath et al., 2022] (Secs. J and M).

3.4 Two analytical results
CSI-noise crossover (Prop. 1). Weighting branches by an estimated quality score helps only as
long as those scores are reliable; when the score itself is too noisy, equal-weight averaging is the
safer choice. The imperfect-CSI MRC literature [Tomiuk et al., 1999, Gao et al., 2003, Simon
and Alouini, 2005] establishes the qualitative collapse of MRC’s advantage as the weighting-noise
variance grows; we sharpen this to a one-line diagnostic in elementary symmetric polynomials
(S1 , S2 ) of the per-branch amplitudes (closed form and linear-Gaussian model in Sec. C.1) and read
the local-versus-cloud reversal of Sec. 5.3 as crossing that threshold; the 15-criterion binary checklist
drives the judge variance back below the crossover and restores the Cauchy-Schwarz ordering.

Iterative-decoding threshold (Prop. 2). Each refinement round either nudges the answer toward a
high-quality target or drags it toward a low-quality one, depending on how the generator-critic loop
responds to small changes in current quality; below a critical sensitivity the loop is self-correcting,

Table 1: Communication → agent mapping. SC: selection combining; MIMO: multiple-input
multiple-output; MCS: modulation and coding scheme. Full table in Sec. A.
                 Comm. Concept                 Agent Analog                           Implementation
                 Diversity (MIMO)              Multi-model/prompt/temp                Diversity ensemble
                 MRC / SC / EGC                Quality-weighted / best / equal        Combining strategies
                 HARQ-CC / IR                  Retry / retry + critic                 Chase / incremental

                 Fountain codes                Sample until confident                 Rateless adaptive
                 FEC block codes               Structured redundancy                  Parity sections
                 ACM / MCS table               Difficulty-aware routing               5-level MCS table


above it extra rounds inject more noise than they remove, and only the best-of-sequence guard
keeps the delivered answer from regressing. We identify the LLM refinement operator with a
one-dimensional quality-update map f at its high-quality fixed point q ∞ , recovering the contraction-
versus-expansion dichotomy of the EXIT-chart threshold [ten Brink, 2001, Richardson and Urbanke,
2001] as |f ′ (q ∞ )| ≶ 1; the dichotomy is consistent with the survivor-cohort transition between 3B,
8B, and 14B generators (Fig. 3). The contraction theorem itself is classical; the contributions are
the modeling identification and the explicit role of the best-of-sequence guard (Remark 4), which
decouples delivered from unguarded quality even below threshold.
Additional parallels to communication theory can be found in Secs. A and M.

4    System design: AGENT C ODEC
AGENT C ODEC implements six communication-theoretic reliability techniques, their variants, as well
as comparable baseline methods within a three-layer architecture: (1) a channel layer wrapping any
OpenAI-compatible LLM endpoint as a stochastic channel with cost/latency instrumentation; (2) a
technique layer implementing all six methods; and (3) an orchestration layer that routes tasks and
collects metrics. A quality scorer serves as the channel estimator; to avoid severe numeric-rating
quantization, the judge answers a weighted binary checklist whose sum yields a fine-grained score
on [0, 1] (sigma-delta style, see Sec. D). Tables 1 and 2 summarize the complete mapping; detailed
hyperparameters are in Sec. N.

Table 2: Taxonomic reduction of comparable prior methods. Every widely cited inference-time
reliability method is recovered as a special case of an AGENT C ODEC technique by setting a single hy-
perparameter; the AGENT C ODEC variant adds one mechanism the prior method omits, so at matched
inference budget it upper-bounds the baseline. See Table 5 for head-to-head evaluation comparisons.

Diversity combining. The framework supports three diversity axes (model, prompt, temperature),
each with SC/MRC/EGC combining; in this paper we focus on model diversity. Each P              branch
generates Yi = Ai (X), is scored qi = q(X, Yi ), and MRC combines with weights wi = qi / j qj
(pseudocode in Sec. N). Compile-time prompt construction [Khattab et al., 2024, Liu et al., 2022,
Rubin et al., 2022, Zhou et al., 2023, Yang et al., 2024] is an orthogonal, amortized-before-inference
source of prompt diversity; composition with decode-time channel combining is left to future
work. HARQ. HARQ-CC generates R independent attempts and combines equally. HARQ-IR


                                                                   5
iteratively refines via a critic that identifies specific errors (new parity); both stop at quality ≥ τ .
Turbo decoding. A generator and critic iteratively exchange extrinsic information: each round,
the critic provides only new feedback (scaled by a damping factor α < 1), mirroring the extrinsic-
only exchange used in classical turbo decoders. Extrinsic scaling, a severity floor, and adaptive
damping prevent the oscillation and over-correction that otherwise plague naive critic–generator
loops (Sec. N). Fountain codes. Samples are generated until a confidence metric exceeds a threshold.
The rateless property automatically adapts cost to task difficulty. FEC. Structured parity sections
(reasoning, self-verification, alternative approach, confidence) are requested alongside the answer
at rates r ∈ {3/4, 1/2, 1/3, 1/4}. A decoder cross-checks sections (syndrome decoding). ACM
routing. A difficulty estimator (pilot signal) selects from a modulation-and-coding-scheme (MCS)
table (MCS-0 through MCS-4) – or in our case uses more advanced techniques, jointly adapting
model and redundancy strategy. Soft-output variants. Three optional soft variants (S OFT-MRC,
S OFT-F OUNTAIN, S OFT-ACM) replace         judge-score CSI with the intrinsic log-likelihood-ratio-like
(LLR-like) signal c(y) = exp L1 t log p(yt | y<t , x) from L per-token log-probabilities (Sec. J).
                                    P
Judge, critic, voter. Judge rates an answer (CSI source); critic rewrites/critiques an answer (extrinsic-
information feedback in iterative decoders); voter clusters multiple answers into equivalence classes
for discrete MRC/majority aggregation. Judge and voter are decoder-side aggregation operators
(post-generation); the critic is part of the iterative decoder loop itself.

5   Experimental Evaluation
5.1 Setup
Tasks. We use two evaluation tiers. Curated tasks: 69 tasks across four categories (QA, reasoning,
creative, code) at three difficulty tiers (hard/very hard/extreme), designed to produce measurable
technique differences at the 3–30B operating points (Sec. B). Standard benchmarks: Hard 100-item
splits of MMLU, GSM8K, HumanEval (Sec. 5.5).
Models and scoring. We evaluate across six channel configurations (full table in Table 7, Sec. E)
spanning local open-weight (3B/8B/14B from Qwen, Llama, Phi, and Gemma families), An-
thropic+OpenAI cloud, and an Ollama-cloud trio (NVIDIA Nemotron-Nano-3 30B and Mistral
Devstral-Small-2 24B as channels, Zhipu GLM-5.1 as judge). We disable/minimize thinking for
reasoning models by default. The standard-benchmark experiment (300 hard items drawn from
MMLU, GSM8K and HumanEval) is run on the Ollama-cloud trio. Local configurations additionally
enable the four soft-output techniques, which require token-level log-probabilities that the Anthropic
API does not currently expose. The judge/synthesizer is deliberately at least as strong as the channel
models, analogous to investing in receiver quality in communications [Proakis and Salehi, 2008]. In
the Anthropic+OpenAI cloud configuration, Haiku 4.5 serves as both channel and judge, confirming
that technique rankings are preserved even without a stronger synthesizer (Sec. E). For verifiable
tasks, blended scoring q = 0.6 · qobj + 0.4 · qjudge mitigates judge unreliability (Sec. D). Thinking
is disabled for all reasoning models to isolate the effects of the studied methods. We report quality
q ∈ [0, 1], coding gain (mean quality improvement over the uncoded baseline), and cost overhead
(normalized cost). Means come with 95% bootstrap confidence intervals; significance via paired
Wilcoxon signed-rank tests on per-task differences. Full model configurations are in Table 7 and a
summary of technique gains across configurations is in Table 8, both in Sec. E.
5.2 Baselines and direct comparisons to prior methods
We re-implement Self-Consistency, Best-of-N , Weighted Best-of-N , Confidence-Informed Self-
Consistency, Mixture-of-Agents, Self-Refine, and Chain-of-Verification as standalone techniques (not
AGENT C ODEC variants), following the canonical recipes in the cited papers, scored on the same tasks
and judge as the AGENT C ODEC runs. Each prior method is a special case of one of three combining
operators (selection / equal-gain / maximal-ratio) over one of two answer spaces (continuous
free-form text or discrete equivalence classes), with three orthogonal knobs: the CSI source ∈ {judge
score, log-probability, trained process reward model, none}, the pool size N , and whether combining
is passive or iterative; full operator-view derivations are in Sec. F and Table 2. Two of our techniques
widen this lattice to a multi-model channel pool: Diversity-SC-N cycles N samples through the
configured channels and selects the argmax-quality sample (collapses to Best-of-N at one channel);
Diversity-MRC-Discrete-N adds a voter clustering step and applies cluster-summed-quality MRC
(collapses to Weighted Best-of-N / CISC at one channel, to Self-Consistency at uniform per-sample
weights). Quality and cost. On the 300-task hard-benchmark split (Ollama-cloud trio, Table 8),
Diversity-MRC-Discrete-N beats CISC by ≈ 9.5 percentage points in quality at ≈ 6% extra cost
and beats Self-Consistency by ≈ 0.9 percentage points at ≈ 17% lower cost; Diversity-SC-N beats


                                                   6
Best-of-N by ≈ 5.2 percentage points at ≈ 2% extra cost; Diversity-EGC beats Mixture-of-Agents
by ≈ 1.8 percentage points at ≈ 47% lower cost. The two prior methods not cleanly dominated
are Self-Refine, which slightly beats HARQ-IR on the Ollama-cloud configurations (by ≈ 3.3
percentage points at ≈ 11% higher cost on the 300-task split, p=0.43, neither dominates on the
quality–cost plane), and Self-Consistency, statistically tied with Diversity-MRC-Discrete-N at the
8B and Ollama-cloud-curated operating points (paired ∆ < 0.7 pp in either direction); the framework
predicts both exceptions (Sec. 5.3 and Prop. 2). FrugalGPT and RouteLLM are degenerate cases
of fountain and ACM respectively (Table 2) and the semantic-KNN router of Sec. 5.4 subsumes
both, beating its closest in-paper proxies at p < 10−4 over 300 × 3 trials (Table 10).

5.3 Quality, cost, and the operating-point dependence
Cost normalization. For a technique π (e.g. Diversity-MRC, HARQ-IR, fountain), per-task cost
overhead (normalized cost) ρ(π, X) := Cost(π, X)/Cost(baseline, X) is analogous to Eb /N0 in
communications (Sec. D); throughout this section we pair the coding gain G(π) = E[qπ (X)] −
E[qbaseline (X)] with the coding-gain efficiency η(π) := G(π)/ρ(π).
Diversity combining. On the cleanest CSI configuration (A+O cloud, Haiku 4.5 + GPT-5-
mini, judge Haiku 4.5), the textbook MRC≥EGC≥SC ordering holds: GMRC = +7.9% ≥
GEGC = +6.2% ≥ GSC = +4.6%. The ordering reverses where the judge-score CSI is noisier:
14B local (GEGC = +11.6% > GMRC = GSC = +6.2%, q0 = 0.746) and Ollama-cloud
(GEGC = +15.1% > GMRC = +11.7% > GSC = +9.4%). This is the CSI-noise crossover of
                                                                               ∗
Prop. 1: residual binary-checklist judge variance puts σw above the critical σw   on the local-and-
Ollama configurations and below it on the cleaner cloud pair. Soft-MRC (intrinsic log-probabilities,
Sec. J) reduces σw further and matches EGC at 14B (0.825 vs. 0.832). Wider-pool variants extend
the picture: at 8B, Diversity-SC-N reaches +33% and Diversity-MRC-Discrete-N reaches +31%.
Full crossover analysis is in Sec. C.1.
Iterative refinement: HARQ and turbo. On 14B, HARQ-CC reaches G = +11.7% (p < 10−3 ),
HARQ-IR +14.0% (p < 10−3 ), and turbo +14.2% (p < 10−5 , paired Wilcoxon W = 56); the
HARQ-CC vs. HARQ-IR gap isolates the value of structured extrinsic feedback at fixed call
budget. The all-tasks running-max rises monotonically iteration-by-iteration (0.726 → 0.853,
Fig. 2). The survivor mean drops after iteration 0, the early-exit artifact predicted by Remark 4: the
best-of-sequence guard ensures Qk ≥ Qk−1 ≥ q0 , but the survivor mean does not. Turbo on weaker
generators traces the threshold of Prop. 2: above at 14B, noisy-flat at 8B, descending at 3B with
only the guard preserving the delivered iterate (Fig. 3; per-scale breakdown in Sec. I).
Forward error correction. On 14B, FEC at r ∈ {3/4, 1/2, 1/3} delivers +7.6% (p < 0.05,
ρ ≈ 3×), +4.3%, +5.1%. r = 3/4 (one extra step-by-step reasoning section) gives the best quality-
cost trade, consistent with chain-of-thought scaling [Wei et al., 2022]; lower rates saturate because
the syndrome-decoding LLM does not exploit additional, less-decorrelated parity sections (Fig. 4).
The best single technique depends on the operating point, and on what one optimizes for.
Table 8 in the Appendix reports raw G. Pairing each G with its ρ and computing η exposes a
recurring asymmetry: the G-leader is rarely the η-leader (Table 9). At the 3B, 8B, and Ollama-cloud
configurations the G-leader is fountain at ρ ∈ [6.3, 9.1]×, while the η-leader is the cheapest combiner
Diversity-SC at ρ ≈ 2×, a 1.5-to-3× improvement in coding gain per unit cost. At 14B, turbo leads
on G but Diversity-EGC matches it on η at ∼ 75% of the cost. Only on the A+O cloud, where
q0 = 0.819 is already near ceiling, does a single technique (HARQ-IR) lead on both axes. This
dependence motivates the cost-aware semantic-KNN ACM router of Sec. 5.4: a single Lagrangian
knob λ moves the deployed router from G-leader to η-leader as described below.
5.4 A cost-aware semantic-nearest-neighbor adaptive-coding-and-modulation router.
We instantiate the routing layer as a non-parametric semantic-nearest-neighbor dispatcher. Each
task x is embedded with BAAI/bge-large-en-v1.5; at deployment, the dispatcher looks up the per-
technique mean quality q̄i (x) and cost c̄i (x) over the k = 20 nearest training-cache neighbors of x
and selects
                      π ⋆ (x; λ) = arg max q̄i (x) − λ · c̄i (x)/c̄baseline (x) ,
                                                                               
                                                                                                  (3)
                                       i∈C

where C is the candidate-technique pool (19 techniques + uncoded baseline in our pool) and λ ≥ 0
is a single Lagrangian knob: λ → 0 recovers a pure quality-maximizer, λ → ∞ collapses to the
cheapest candidate. The dispatch rule is fully determined by the cache snapshot and the embedding

model; the same router instance is therefore deployable across cost regimes by adjusting λ, with no

The router at λ=3 beats the cross-validated fixed-best policy by 6.8% in mean quality (paired
Wilcoxon p < 10−13 , Cohen’s dz = 0.47, win rate 59.7% at the per-task level) at matched normal-
ized cost; Fig. 1 plots the quality–cost plane and shows that the entire λ-sweep sits above every fixed
technique on the empirical Pareto frontier. Sweeping the knob to λ=38 instance still matches FB-CV
in mean quality while running at 55.7% lower normalized cost per task than fixed-best. The headline
numbers are computed on 900 trial-task observations (n=300 tasks × 3 independent generation
repeats per task) with seed-aware bootstrap resampling that propagates within-task generation vari-
ance into every confidence interval. The full Pareto sweep and additional comparators (per-category
fixed-best, scalar-difficulty bins, hand-coded ACM, the in-sample feasibility ceiling) are in Table 10
and Fig. 1. Classical maximal-ratio combining is information-theoretically optimal under high signal-


                                                                                                    8
to-noise ratio with perfect channel-state information [Proakis and Salehi, 2008], but that optimality is
conditional on a fixed channel regime: allowing the regime itself to vary across tasks and dispatching
task-by-task strictly dominates any single fixed combiner on the empirical quality–cost frontier.
5.5 Category analysis and standard-benchmark validation
On the 14B-curated tasks, category dominance varies by technique (per-category bars in Fig. 16;
full per-configuration breakdown in Sec. G): hybrid retransmission with incremental redundancy
leads on question answering (q = 0.861) and reasoning (0.869), maximal-ratio combining on code
(0.866), and equal-gain combining / turbo on creative (0.822 / 0.806), as expected from the channel
model: iterative techniques excel where concrete errors can be identified, diversity combining where
independent attempts explore different solution paths. To rule out curation artifacts we re-evaluate
on hard-stratified splits of n = 100 items per dataset of GSM8K, MMLU (abstract_algebra),
and HumanEval, constructed via published structural proxies (step count, subject difficulty, doctest
count); technique rankings match the curated-task results on all three (per-dataset bars and Pareto
scatters in Figures 36 and 37, full breakdown in Sec. G). Three validation experiments rule out
methodology artifacts: an inter-judge ablation preserves the technique ordering despite absolute-score
variation; a judge-in-loop confound check shows that judge-free decoders still win about 39% of
oracle assignments on the 8B split, ruling out the strong borrowing hypothesis; and a synthesis-
integrity audit (Sec. L) finds zero contamination across five controlled tests, so the gains attributed to
combining cannot be the synthesizer solving the task on its own.
6   Discussion
Technique selection guidelines. The framework gives principled criteria: diversity combining
for independent-error tasks (question answering); HARQ with incremental redundancy when errors
are identifiable by a critic; turbo when iterative refinement compounds (reasoning); fountain when
difficulty is unknown a priori; forward error correction for tasks with checkable consistency (code);
and the cost-aware semantic-nearest-neighbor router for heterogeneous task streams when both
quality and cost matter. Table 3 sharpens the last point: a single adaptive router with one Lagrangian
knob λ strictly dominates any single fixed combiner (including textbook-optimal maximal-ratio
combining) on the empirical quality–cost frontier; classical optimality results assume a fixed channel
regime, while real task streams traverse multiple regimes and reward per-task allocation.
Generator bottleneck and the decoding threshold. Iterative techniques are limited by generator
capacity, not critic quality, mirroring the iterative-decoding threshold [ten Brink, 2001]: Prop. 2 writes
the threshold as a contraction–expansion transition of the per-iteration quality-update map f at its
high-quality fixed point q ∞ . Our three local model scales trace this transition (Table 8, Fig. 3, Sec. I):
refinement is coherent at 14B, noisy-flat at 8B, and net-destructive at 3B with only the guard preserving
monotonicity. Operational implication: deploy iterative techniques only when the generator is known
to be above threshold; otherwise prefer passive diversity [Proakis and Salehi, 2008] or routing.
Limitations and open directions. Limitations are detailed in Sec. I: residual judge-score noise from
the binary-checklist scorer; scoring overhead on easy tasks; non-stationarity and a dual-role judge
confound (judge-free decoders still win about 39% of oracle assignments, leaving a judge-strength
ablation open); and curated-task selection bias, addressed by the standard-benchmark validation.
Open directions include bounding I(X; Y ) for LLM channels, calibrating λ from a user-supplied
cost constraint, and enriching router features beyond sentence embeddings.

7   Conclusion
Sampling an autoregressive language model at temperature T realizes a discrete stochastic channel,
and that identity makes the communication-theoretic reliability toolkit transfer with minimal
adaptation: a closed-form channel-state-information crossover (Prop. 1) above which equal-gain
combining beats noisy maximal-ratio combining, and a fixed-point criterion (Prop. 2) identifying
LLM refinement with an EXIT recursion, the contraction-versus-expansion dichotomy of which
is consistent with the 3B/8B/14B transition we observe. The cost-aware semantic-nearest-neighbor
router beats every fixed technique on the Ollama-cloud quality-cost frontier (6.8% quality lead at
matched cost; 55.7% lower cost at matched quality): classical maximal-ratio optimality is conditional
on a fixed regime, whereas real workloads traverse multiple regimes and reward per-task allocation
through a single Lagrangian knob. A substantially larger design space (interleaving, structured
forward error correction, joint source-channel coding; Sec. K) and operational capacity bounds,
λ-calibration, and richer router features remain.

Appendix

A     Primer on communication systems

This appendix provides an accessible introduction to the communication-theoretic concepts used
throughout the paper. Readers familiar with digital communications may skip this section; it is
intended for the ML/AI audience.

A.1   The communication problem

The fundamental problem in communication is: how do you send a message reliably over a noisy
channel? A transmitter sends a signal, the channel corrupts it (adds noise, causes fading, drops bits),
and the receiver must recover the original message. Shannon’s channel coding theorem [Shannon,
1948] establishes that reliable communication is possible up to a fundamental limit called the channel
capacity C, provided the transmitter adds sufficient structured redundancy.
A prompt (message) is sent through a stochastic model (channel) whose transition probability is
pθ (y | x), which may produce hallucinations, omissions, or reasoning errors (noise), and the user
(receiver) must extract a correct answer (decoded message). As formalized in Sec. 3, the LLM’s
conditional distribution satisfies the definition of a channel transition probability.

A.2   Signal-to-Noise Ratio (SNR)

SNR measures how much “useful signal” there is relative to “noise” in a received signal. Higher SNR
means cleaner reception and lower error rates. In wireless systems, SNR depends on transmit power,
distance, and interference.
Agent analog: The LLM channel’s intrinsic noise is its conditional entropy H = H(Y |X), the
per-token uncertainty in the model’s output distribution (Eq. (2)). Lower H means higher channel
quality (analogous to higher SNR). In practice, we estimate channel quality via a quality score
q ∈ [0, 1] produced by an LLM judge, a noisy external channel estimator. Just as wireless engineers
measure SNR to decide how to process a received signal, our framework measures output quality to
decide how to process agent outputs.

A.3   Diversity and combining

In wireless systems, a signal may travel via multiple independent paths (e.g., multiple antennas in
MIMO systems). Each path experiences different noise, so if one path is in a deep fade, another may
still be strong. The receiver combines these paths to improve reliability. Three standard strategies
exist:
       • Selection combining (SC): Pick the single best path. Simple but discards useful information
         from other paths.
       • Equal-gain combining (EGC): Average all paths equally. Better than SC but suboptimal if
         path qualities differ.
       • Maximal-ratio combining (MRC): Weight each path proportionally to its SNR, then
         combine. This is provably optimal under independent noise.
With d independent paths, the probability that all are bad drops as SNR−d , an exponential improve-
ment called diversity gain.
Agent analog: Generate d independent agent outputs (using different models, prompts, or temper-
atures) and combine them. SC → pick the best output; EGC → synthesize all equally; MRC →
synthesize with quality-weighted emphasis. Independence can be achieved through model diversity
(spatial), prompt rephrasing (frequency), or temperature variation (time). Our experiments focus
on spatial diversity; frequency and time diversity are supported by the framework and left to future
evaluation. The frequency axis as defined here is decode-time prompt rephrasing applied per request;
an orthogonal line of work realizes prompt diversity at compile time, either by treating prompts as
compilable artifacts with searchable instructions, demonstrations, and pipelines [Khattab et al., 2024],
by retrieving or learning per-task in-context examples [Liu et al., 2022, Rubin et al., 2022], or by


                                                  A1
LLM-in-the-loop instruction optimization [Zhou et al., 2023, Yang et al., 2024]. These methods amor-
tize their search before inference and produce a single optimized prompt at decode time; composing
them with the decode-time channel combiners studied here is a natural — and untested — direction
we do not pursue in this paper.

A.4   Automatic Repeat Request (ARQ) and Hybrid ARQ

ARQ is the simplest reliability mechanism: if the receiver detects errors, it requests retransmission.
Hybrid ARQ (HARQ) combines this with forward error correction for better efficiency:
       • HARQ-CC (Chase Combining): Retransmit the exact same data. The receiver soft-
         combines all copies, improving SNR through repetition.
       • HARQ-IR (Incremental Redundancy): Each retransmission sends new parity information.
         The decoder accumulates information across rounds, converging more efficiently than CC.
HARQ-IR is strictly more efficient: each round adds new information rather than repeating what was
already sent.
Agent analog: HARQ-CC → retry the same prompt and combine all attempts. HARQ-IR → use
a critic to identify specific errors, then retry with that feedback (new information) appended to the
prompt. The quality threshold τ serves as the target error rate; iteration stops when quality exceeds τ .

A.5   Turbo Codes and Iterative Decoding

Turbo codes [Berrou et al., 1993] revolutionized communications by approaching Shannon’s limit.
They use two simple encoders/decoders that iteratively exchange “extrinsic information,” soft relia-
bility estimates that each decoder produces for the other. Above a decoder-specific SNR threshold,
this iterative exchange can produce a waterfall effect in classical channels: error rates drop sharply
once a critical information threshold is crossed. We do not claim this waterfall behavior carries
over quantitatively to LLM channels; our turbo implementation aims for the qualitative benefit of
extrinsic-only feedback, not a bit-error-rate curve.
Agent analog: Two LLM agents (generator and critic) iteratively exchange outputs. The generator
produces an answer; the critic provides structured feedback (extrinsic information); the generator
refines. Each round, both agents condition on the other’s latest output, mirroring the extrinsic
information exchange in turbo decoding.

A.6   Fountain (Rateless) Codes

Traditional codes have a fixed rate r = k/n, chosen before transmission. Fountain codes [Luby,
2002] instead produce a potentially unlimited stream of encoded symbols. The receiver collects
symbols until it has enough to decode, automatically adapting to unknown or time-varying channel
conditions without rate negotiation.
Agent analog: Generate agent outputs one at a time, checking quality after each. Stop as soon as
accumulated outputs are sufficient to synthesize a high-quality answer. No need to pre-specify how
many samples are needed; the system adapts to task difficulty at runtime.

A.7   Forward Error Correction (FEC)

FEC adds structured redundancy to a message before transmission so the receiver can detect and
correct errors without retransmission. The code rate r = k/n controls the redundancy: lower rates
add more redundancy (more protection, higher cost). The receiver performs syndrome decoding,
checking parity constraints to locate and fix errors.
Agent analog: Instead of asking for just an answer, prompt the model to also produce structured
“parity” sections: step-by-step reasoning (parity 1), self-verification (parity 2), confidence assessment
(parity 3), and an alternative approach (parity 4). A decoder agent then cross-checks these sections
for internal consistency (analogous to syndrome decoding) and corrects any detected errors in the
main answer. The code rate controls how many parity sections are requested.


                                                   A2
A.8   Adaptive Coding and Modulation (ACM)

In wireless systems (LTE, 5G), the transmitter continuously estimates channel quality and selects the
best modulation and coding scheme (MCS). Good channels get high-rate, low-redundancy schemes
(high throughput); bad channels get low-rate, high-redundancy schemes (high reliability). This is
formalized as a table mapping SNR ranges to MCS levels.
Agent analog: Estimate task difficulty (the “channel quality”), then route to the most cost-efficient
technique. Easy tasks use cheap, low-redundancy methods (single fast model); hard tasks use
expensive, high-redundancy methods (turbo decoding with a frontier model). This is the key to
optimizing the cost–quality tradeoff: not every task needs the same level of effort.

A.9   Summary of mappings

Table 4 consolidates the communication-to-agent concept correspondences used throughout the paper,
from the channel transition probability identity through the soft-decision recipe to the rate–reliability
trade-off.

                         Table 4: Communication ↔ Agent concept mapping.
                   Communication Concept            Agent Analog
                   Transition prob. W (y|x)         pθ (y | x) (exact identity)
                   Channel                          LLM model
                   Noise variance σ 2               Temperature T
                   Noise (AWGN, fading)             Hallucination, omission, errors
                   Conditional entropy H(Y |X)      Per-token uncertainty of LLM
                   Received signal                  Agent output
                   Soft-decision output (LLR)       Token log-probabilities
                   SNR / channel quality            −H(Aθ , x) (neg. cond. entropy)
                   Channel estimator (CSI)          Sigma-delta LLM-judge scorer
                   Diversity (MIMO)                 Multi-model/prompt/temp sampling
                   Combining (SC/EGC/MRC)           Output selection/synthesis
                   ARQ retransmission               Prompt retry
                   HARQ-IR parity bits              Critic feedback
                   Turbo extrinsic info             Generator–critic exchange
                   Decoding threshold               Min. model capacity for convergence
                   Fountain symbols                 Incremental samples
                   FEC parity bits                  Reasoning/verification sections
                   Code rate r = k/n                Redundancy level
                   ACM / MCS table                  Task-difficulty routing
                   I(X; Y ), capacity C             Mutual info., max quality at cost

Full task specifications for all 36 benchmark tasks are available in the supplementary material. Each
task includes: prompt text, reference answer (where applicable), difficulty rating, and evaluation
criteria.

C     Linear-Combiner justification for the MRC weighting
This appendix reproduces the linear-combiner argument that motivates the weighting in Remark 2; it
is not a proof that this weighting is end-to-end optimal for a nonlinear LLM synthesizer.
Consider d agent outputs Y1 , . . . , Yd with quality scores q1 , . . . , qd , treated as independent signal
components with additive noise of variance σi2 and signal amplitude proportional to qi . For a linear
combiner with weights wi summing to a fixed constant, the combined SNR is maximized when
wi ∝ qi /σi2 . Under the further simplifying assumption that noise variance is approximately equal
across branches (so σi2 ≈ σ 2 ), this reduces to wi∗ ∝ qi , giving the normalized form
                                                        qi
                                           wiMRC = Pd          .                                         (4)
                                                       j=1 qj


                                                    A3
This directly parallels the classical MRC result in AWGN channels, where optimal weights are
proportional to per-branch SNR [Proakis and Salehi, 2008].
Caveats specific to the agent setting. (i) The LLM synthesizer is not a linear combiner; increasing
wi biases the synthesizer’s attention toward input i, but does not literally scale Yi additively. (ii) qi is
a noisy LLM-judge estimate, not an unbiased SNR. (iii) Branch outputs Yi are correlated through
shared training data, which reduces the effective diversity order deff . The empirical ordering in
Sec. 5.3 (where EGC outperforms MRC) is consistent with these caveats being active; see Remark 3
and [Gao et al., 2003, Simon and Alouini, 2005].

Testable signature. The crossover variance (6) is written in the per-branch amplitudes ai , which
we do not measure directly: the judge supplies âi rather than ai , and the noise floor σ is itself a
model-fitted quantity. The proposition’s empirical content is therefore not the numerical value of
  ∗2
σw                                                               MRC-versus-EGC ordering reversal
     for any one configuration, but the signature it predicts: a \
whenever the judge’s effective resolution coarsens, and a return to the Cauchy-Schwarz ordering
                                                                                          2
once the resolution is sharpened, with the crossing happening at a single value of σw       rather than
gradually. Both directions of this signature, and the single-crossing structure, are observed in the
experiments below (and summarized in Table 8); the proposition is falsifiable by, e.g., a configuration
where coarsening the judge improves MRC
                                      \ relative to EGC, or where the two combiners cross more
than once as the judge is varied. We have not seen such a configuration in this paper; finding one
would refute the linear-Gaussian idealization underlying (5).

Attribution. The first-order expectation (5) for the MRC SNR under independent Gaussian channel-
state-information errors is the textbook imperfect-CSI MRC analysis. The Cauchy–Schwarz com-
parison γMRC ≥ γEGC at perfect CSI is in any classical diversity-combining reference [Proakis and
Salehi, 2008], and the qualitative collapse of the MRC advantage as the CSI-noise variance grows is
the imperfect-CSI MRC line beginning with Tomiuk et al. [1999] and continued by Gao et al. [2003]
and Simon and Alouini [2005, Ch. 9]. Our specific contribution is the closed-form crossover variance
(6) as a one-line diagnostic in (S1 , S2 ) form, and its application to the LLM-judge CSI-quantization
regime: the binary-checklist judge of Remark 3 pushes σw across the threshold and the empirical
EGC ≻ MRC \ reversal of Sec. 5.3 reverts to the Cauchy–Schwarz ordering.
Implication for the empirical reversal. The pre-sigma-delta judge produced ∼ 33 unique values
across 516 measurements (Remark 3), so the per-branch CSI quantization step was on the order of the
                                                                             ∗
spread of true amplitudes themselves — σw comparable to or exceeding σw        . The 15-criterion binary
checklist enlarges the effective level count by roughly a factor 2 /33 ≈ 103 , sharply reducing σw
                                                                    15

and pushing operation back below the crossover, where Cauchy–Schwarz restores the MRC ⪰ EGC
ordering. Intrinsic-logprob CSI (Sec. J) reduces σw further still, since the weight is generated by the
same process that produced y and is not subject to judge quantization.
Caveats. (i) The first-order expectation neglects the covariance between the (random) numerator and
denominator; the exact ratio expectation is intractable in closed form but the qualitative crossover
is preserved [Gao et al., 2003, Simon and Alouini, 2005].P(ii) The synthesizer is not a literal linear
combiner; the result motivates the heuristic wi = qi / j qj but does not establish end-to-end
optimality for an LLM combiner. (iii) Branch correlation introduces an additional EGC-favoring
term not captured here.

C.2   Coding Gain and Channel Capacity

For a technique π, the coding gain G(π) = E[qπ (X)]−E[qbaseline (X)] measures improvement over un-
coded transmission; coding gain efficiency η = G(π)/(Cost(π)/Cost(baseline)) normalizes by cost
overhead. Since pθ (y | x) is a well-defined conditional distribution, I(X; Y ) = H(Y ) − H(Y |X)
exists, and Shannon’s coding theorem applies [Shannon, 1948, Cover and Thomas, 2006]. We define
the operational quality capacity Q∗ (C) = supπ∈ΠC E[qπ (X)] over technique configurations ΠC
with cost ≤ C. Computing the true C remains open (Sec. M), but I(X; Y ) formalizes diminishing
diversity returns under correlation (subadditivity of mutual information).


D     Experimental methodology details

Hardware and infrastructure. All local experiments (3B/8B/14B configurations) were run on a
single workstation: Intel Core i9-13900K (24 cores), NVIDIA GeForce RTX 3090 (24 GB VRAM),
32 GB DDR5 RAM, Ubuntu 24.04 LTS. Open-weight models are served via Ollama with default
quantization (typically 4-bit GGUF for 14B models to fit within 24 GB VRAM). Cloud experiments


                                                  A5
(Claude Haiku 4.5, GPT-5-mini) use the respective provider APIs. No multi-GPU or cluster resources
are required; all results are reproducible on consumer hardware with a single GPU.

Cost normalization. Raw cost conflates task difficulty with technique overhead: a hard task
requiring five HARQ rounds costs more than an easy task needing one, but appears in the “high cost,
low quality” quadrant, misleadingly suggesting that spending more degrades quality. We therefore
normalize cost per task against its baseline, defining cost overhead:
                                                    Cost(π, X)
                                    ρ(π, X) =                                                  (7)
                                                Cost(baseline, X)
This is analogous to the Eb /N0 normalization in communications, where performance is plotted
against energy per information bit rather than total transmitted power, enabling fair comparison
across channels with different noise levels.

Computational efficiency and scaling with dataset size. The dominant cost of every AGENT-
C ODEC technique is LLM inference, and the cost overhead ρ of (7) is the quantity reported throughout
the experiments. Each technique scales linearly in its tunable budget knob and is independent across
tasks: diversity combining at branch count d uses d generator calls, d judge calls, and one synthesizer
call; HARQ-CC/IR and turbo use up to K=5 rounds with one generator and one critic call per
round (with adaptive early-exit when qk ≥ τ ); fountain stops adaptively in [Nmin , Nmax ] = [2, 10]
samples; FEC at code rate r uses 1/r generator calls plus one syndrome-decoding call. The deployed
semantic-nearest-neighbor router adds one BAAI/bge-large-en-v1.5 embedding per task and a k=20
nearest-neighbor lookup over the training cache, which is O(Ncache ) with the naive scan used here
and O(log Ncache ) with any standard approximate-nearest-neighbor index; for Ncache ≤ 300 as in
Sec. 5, both the embedding and the lookup are negligible against the seconds-scale latency of a
single generator call. Training the parametric router ablations (ACM R IDGE, ACM L EARNED) com-
pletes in seconds on a single CPU core via closed-form L2 -ridge or a small multinomial logit per
fold (Sec. E.1). For a corpus of N tasks, total inference compute therefore scales as Θ(N ) (each
task is processed independently) and the one-time cache-embedding cost as Θ(N ); in our cloud
configurations, wall-clock is throughput-bound by provider rate limits rather than by local compute,
while in the local Ollama configurations it is bound by single-GPU generator throughput on the
workstation of the previous paragraph. Because each task is processed independently, we do not
anticipate qualitative changes in any technique’s behavior at substantially larger N ; whether the
router’s quality lead over the cross-validated best fixed technique (Table 3) sharpens or flattens as the
cache grows, and whether an indexed neighbor structure is needed in deployment, are left to future
evaluation.

Weighted binary-checklist judge (“sigma-delta-style”). A naive numeric-rating judge (“score
this response from 0 to 1”) is severely quantized for small judge models: an initial Gemma-3 12B
judge produced only ∼33 unique values across 516 measurements (∼5-bit resolution), with 39% of
scores collapsing onto a single value. Under such coarse CSI, MRC’s quality weighting amplifies
estimation noise and the theoretical MRC ≥ EGC ordering collapses (Remark 3). We instead elicit
15 weighted binary criteria covering correctness, completeness, reasoning, and presentation (different
criterion set with and without a reference answer) and compute the score as the weighted sum of
yes-answers. Binary yes/no decisions are far more reliable for small LLMs than numeric ratings, and
15 weighted 1-bit measurements yield up to 215 distinct score values.2 All experiments in this paper
use this scorer.

Blended scoring. For tasks with objectively verifiable answers, blended scoring combines auto-
mated verification with the LLM judge:
                                  qblended = 0.6 · qobj + 0.4 · qjudge                            (8)
where qobj ∈ [0, 1] is the weighted proportion of regex-verified factual checks that pass, and qjudge
is the LLM judge score. In controlled tests, the LLM judge scored a 6/7-correct response at 0.60
while qobj = 0.86; blended scoring reduces this gap from 0.26 to <0.10. This approach mitigates the
known unreliability of LLM-as-judge evaluators [Zheng et al., 2023, Gu et al., 2026] while retaining
flexibility for open-ended tasks where objective scoring is unavailable.
    2We label this design sigma-delta-style by loose analogy with oversampled Σ∆ ADCs [Norsworthy et al., 1996], which

trade bit depth for sample count. Our scorer is strictly a weighted-binary classifier; it lacks the noise-shaping feedback loop
and temporal oversampling of a true Σ∆ modulator, so the analogy is qualitative and design-motivating rather than structural.


                                                             A6
Synthesis integrity. For MRC/EGC combining, synthesis contamination is a methodological
concern: the synthesizer model may inject its own knowledge rather than faithfully merging inputs.
Quality gains attributed to combining would then reflect the synthesizer solving the task independently,
invalidating the diversity framework. Our constrained synthesis prompt (“output must contain ONLY
information from the responses above”) combined with low temperature (T = 0.1) effectively prevents
this. Five controlled tests confirm zero contamination; full details in Sec. L.

E.1   ACM oracle-gap decomposition: methodology and full table

Setup. To address the calibration-leakage potential concerns for the semantic-nearest-neighbor
router (ACM S EM KNN, Sec. 5.4), all routing policies are calibrated and scored under 5-fold cross-
validation (CV) stratified by category. The evaluation cache is the Ollama-cloud configuration
with n=300 standard-benchmark tasks generated nrep =3 independent times each (900 trial-task
observations), 240/60 train/test split per fold, and 4000 bootstrap samples for confidence intervals;
the same fold partition is applied across repeats so within-task generation variance is absorbed
by the bootstrap. The KNN router is fit on the BAAI/bge-large-en-v1.5 embeddings of the train
prompts, with kernel size k=20 and a single Lagrangian knob λ controlling the cost penalty. Five
additional routers serve as comparators: F IXED B EST (CV-deployable, abbreviated FB-CV: argmax
mean quality on train), C ATEGORY B EST (per-category argmax), S CALAR D IFFICULTY B INS (per-bin
argmax over quantile edges of the train pilot-difficulty distribution), and two parametric ablations
of ACM S EM KNN: ACM R IDGE (L2 -ridge regression per technique on the extended feature set
[1, d, 1cat , log#words, 1has_code , 1has_math , 1has_numbers , 1has_question , log#sentences, |word|], dispatch
by argmax predicted quality) and ACM L EARNED (L2 -regularized multinomial logit on [1, d, 1cat ]
predicting the per-task argmax-technique label; also shipped as acm_learned, see Sec. E.3). The
hand-coded ACM bins committed in production are evaluated twice: simulated, by applying the
routing rules to the cached per-technique quality, and realized, by reading the actual acm.json
quality under ACM’s profile-specific round and branch counts. The realized-vs-oracle gap admits the

Table 6: Unified operator view of inference-time reliability methods. Each cell names the prior

is genuinely orthogonal. The same router instance traverses the empirical Pareto frontier with one

oracle cost; the intermediate λ=20 (0.851, $0.00381) loses only ≈ 2.3 percentage points relative to

branch counts deliver a small integration win rather than a lossy operating-point dial. The residual
quality gap to oracle is therefore information-bound: closing the +0.034 information gap requires
either richer features (response length, pilot-branch agreement, category-conditioned log-probability)
or a stronger channel-state-information signal than the pilot’s mean log-probability.


Cross-validation fully addresses any potential concerns about calibration leakage. Every
test-fold task is dispatched using nearest-neighbor means computed only over its 240 train-fold
neighbors; test-fold per-technique scores are never seen at fit time, and the same fold partition is
applied across the nrep =3 repeats so within-task generation variance is absorbed by the bootstrap
rather than by the train/test split. The deployed router scores 0.874 [0.859, 0.888], ≈ 5.5 percentage
points above FB-CV (paired Wilcoxon p<10−17 , Cohen’s dz =0.45, win rate 59%); against the
simulated-ACM hand-coded bins the lead is +0.179 (p<10−46 , dz =0.97, win rate 93%). The two
parametric ablations on identical splits agree: ACM R IDGE scores 0.849 and ACM L EARNED scores
0.846, both within bootstrap noise of the deployed kernel and both well above FB-CV, ruling out
the possibility that the lead is an artifact of one router class. FB-CV itself collapses to two distinct
techniques across the five folds (fountain on 80% of tasks, self_refine on 20%), the structural
reason a single fixed technique cannot match a router on this split.


Per-category breakdown. The deployed kernel sits essentially at the LOO ceiling on every pop-
ulated category: question answering, 0.800 vs. feasible 0.795 (n=100); reasoning, 0.960 vs. 0.969
(n=100); code, 0.862 vs. 0.868 (n=100). Most of the residual headroom to oracle is therefore
information-bound rather than dispatcher-bound: oracle reaches 0.841 on QA and 0.913 on code, so
closing the per-category gap requires richer features or a better CSI signal, not a richer router class.
The creative category is empty (n=0) on the standard-benchmark split.

Figure 5: Per-category quality–cost Pareto scatter on the 14B local configuration (n=69 curated
tasks; one panel per task category). The curated tasks cover four categories (question answering,
reasoning, creative, code); each panel plots per-task quality versus normalized cost overhead ρ
for every technique, with the per-technique mean shown as a large marker with a 95% bootstrap
confidence interval. The location of the cost-aware adaptive-coding-and-modulation router (ACM)
within each panel illustrates the per-category Pareto trade-off that the global frontier of Fig. 1
aggregates.

Pick-frequency structure of the λ-sweep. The dispatch profile of each router (Fig. 9) is informative
in its own right. At λ=3 the deployed kernel uses 12 distinct techniques, with no single share above
25.3% (harq_cc, 76/300); the next four shares are self_refine (20.7%), diversity_sc_N
(19.7%), fountain (12.7%), and best_of_n (7.7%). As λ rises the dispatch contracts: at λ=20 it
picks four techniques dominated by diversity_sc (186/300, 62.0%); at the cost extreme λ=38
it collapses to three, with diversity_sc (50.3%) and the uncoded baseline (32.0%) covering
≈ 82% of tasks while self_refine (17.7%) handles the residual hard ones, exactly the cost-
penalty behavior the Lagrangian knob predicts: prune expensive variants on easy tasks where a
cheap multi-model diversity operator (or the uncoded baseline) is already near-oracle. The two
parametric ablations dispatch differently still: ACM R IDGE concentrates on self_refine (32.7%)
and diversity_sc_N (26.3%) across seven techniques, while ACM L EARNED collapses harder,
picking five techniques led by self_refine (53.7%) and best_of_n (35.0%). The KNN router’s
quality lead therefore co-occurs with a markedly more diverse dispatch profile, consistent with the
embedding kernel exploiting per-task structure that the linear ablations cannot.
Decomposition results for the remaining channel configurations of Table 7 will be appended to this
section as their caches become available; the n=300 Ollama-cloud result is the primary headline
because it is the largest evaluated split, includes the most candidate techniques, and exercises the
cost-aware Pareto sweep end-to-end.

E.2   Full per-policy semantic-nearest-neighbor table

The “feasible” row in Table 10 is the leave-one-out in-sample upper bound on the same router class
(semantic-nearest-neighbor applied to the full cache); it bounds what additional features or richer
pools could in principle deliver without changing the dispatch rule. The router at λ=3 is within
