Figure 9: Per-policy technique pick frequency on the n=300 Ollama-cloud cache (legend on the right
enumerates the 20 candidate techniques; each group on the horizontal axis is one of the router policies
of Table 10; bar height is the count, out of 300 tasks, of dispatches that selected that technique under
that policy; pick frequencies are stable across the nrep =3 generation repeats so the bars are reported
at the task level). Three structural facts are visible at a glance. (i) Knob-driven contraction of support:
the cost-aware semantic-nearest-neighbor sweep λ ∈ {1, 5, 10, 20, 25, 30, 35, 40} moves from a fan
of 11 distinct techniques at λ=1 (largest is self_refine, 69/300=23%) to a four-technique regime
at λ=20 (diversity_sc 125/300, harq_cc 70/300, self_refine 62/300, baseline 36/300)
and finally, at the cost-extreme λ=40, to a regime in which the uncoded baseline (148/300,
49%) and diversity_sc (94/300, 31%) cover ≈ 81% of tasks with self_refine (54/300, 18%)
handling the residual hard ones. (ii) Router-class signature: the two parametric ablations (ACM R IDGE,
ACM L EARNED) are visibly different from the deployed kernel and from each other. ACM R IDGE
on extended features uses ten techniques with self_refine dominant (100/300); ACM L EARNED
(the legacy multinomial logit on [1, d, 1cat ]) collapses to only four techniques. (iii) Fixed-best
degeneracy: the cross-validated fixed-best policy uses only three techniques across the five folds
(diversity_sc_N and self_refine on 120/300 tasks each, fountain on 60/300), the structural
reason a single fixed technique cannot match a router on this split. The two right-most groups are the
in-sample feasibility rows (feasible_semknn, feasible_in_sample); their support is the upper
bound a kernel-based or linear router could use absent the train/test split.
E.3               The acm_learned technique

acm_learned serves two roles in this paper, neither of which is the headline ACM result on the
Ollama-cloud cache. First, it is a parametric ablation of the deployed semantic-nearest-neighbor
router (Sec. 5.4): identical CV protocol, identical candidate set, but a multinomial logit on [1, d, 1cat ]
in place of an embedding-kernel mean. Its CV mean of 0.851 on the n=300 tasks × 3-repeat split
(900 trial-task observations; Table 10) lies ≈ 3.4 percentage points below the deployed kernel and
well above the cross-validated fixed-best policy, ruling out the hypothesis that the kernel’s +0.071
lead over fixed-best is an artifact of one router class. Second, it is the automation argument: bin edges
that an off-the-shelf logit can recover from any cache do not need to be hand-tuned per deployment.
We ship acm_learned as a standalone technique that deploys the multinomial logit in place of the
hand-coded bins. Training is a single command, reproducible from any cache directory:
python -m scripts.train_acm_router <cache_dir> \
       --out results/router_weights/acm_learned_<tag>.json
The output JSON contains the weights, the feature specification, the training-time L2
penalty, the training-set accuracy and mean quality, and the K-fold out-of-fold mean
quality together with the per-fold values.           The training script and the runtime class
agentcodec.techniques.ACMLearnedRouter share the feature definition [1, d, 1cat ] and the
candidate-technique list, so a weights file trained on one cache is directly deployable on any bench-


                                                                                                                                                                             A15
mark that uses the same dispatcher vocabulary. Per-technique dispatch parameters (rounds, branches,
code rate) are module-level defaults matched to the standalone-technique cache settings; they can be
overridden per-experiment via the acm_learned_dispatch_defaults key in the YAML.
The acm_learned entry in Sec. E.1 reports its out-of-fold mean quality, not an in-sample fit. The
comparison against the deployed ACM S EM KNN router is therefore methodologically symmetric:
both use identical CV folds, identical candidate set, and identical per-technique dispatch, and differ
only in how task features map to a technique label, multinomial logit on [1, d, 1cat ] versus a non-
parametric kernel mean over the 20-nearest BAAI/bge-large-en-v1.5 prompt embeddings.

F    Operator-view derivations
Direct empirical comparison. We implement Self-Consistency, Self-Refine, and Chain-of-
Verification as standalone baselines (not AGENT C ODEC techniques) following the canonical recipes
in the cited papers, using the same generator, judge, and task set as the AGENT C ODEC runs. Budget
matching: Self-Consistency and Best-of-N use N = 5 samples, Self-Refine uses 3 refinement rounds,
and Chain-of-Verification uses the 4-step canonical pipeline. The AGENT C ODEC counterparts are
configured at the same call budget (N = 5 for Diversity MRC, K = 5 for HARQ-IR/turbo, rate r = 0.5
for FEC). Table 5 reports the paired-difference results; statistical tests use paired Wilcoxon signed-
rank at α = 0.05. The consistent direction of the effect is evidence that each added mechanism yields
a measurable and not merely cosmetic improvement over the prior method.

Diversity-SC-N is Selection Combining at wider pool size. Canonical Best-of-N [Cobbe et al.,
2021b, Lightman et al., 2024] and our Diversity SC operator differ only in pool size and sample source:
BoN draws N independent samples from one policy model via temperature sampling; Diversity SC
(N = 2) draws one sample from each of two diverse generators. Both apply the same selection
operator arg maxi qi . The natural generalization is Diversity-SC-N : cycle N samples through the
full set of configured channels (a multi-model diversity pool sampled to depth N ) and select the
argmax. Diversity-SC-N collapses to canonical BoN when only one channel is configured, and to
ordinary Diversity SC when N = |channels|. At 8B with N = 5 we observe qDiv-SC-N = 0.803 vs.
qSC,N =2 = 0.746, a +0.057 gain attributable to two compounding effects: the N -branch SC gain
GSC (N ) ∝ log N for Rayleigh-faded branches with perfect CSI, plus cross-model diversity that
a single-policy BoN cannot exploit. Canonical BoN with a trained PRM [Lightman et al., 2024]
is the single-model, learned-CSI instance of the same selection operator; Diversity-SC-N is the
multi-model, judge-CSI instance. The operator is identical in both cases, and the free parameters
in the SC-family reduce to the pool size N , the selector s (LLM judge, PRM, or logprob), and the
sample source (single policy vs. multi-model pool).

Diversity-MRC-Discrete-N is MRC on the discrete answer space. Canonical Weighted Best-of-
N [Taubenfeld et al., 2025] (equivalently, confidence-informed self-consistency or CISC) extends
BoN by partitioning the N samples into semantic equivalence classes and weighting each class by the
sum of its members’ judge scores before selecting. This is exactly Diversity MRC transposed to the
discrete answer space: clusters replace branches, cluster-summed judge scores replace per-branch
SNR, and the cluster with the highest weighted sum is selected with its top-q member returned as
the combined output. Two degenerate cases recover familiar prior methods: if every sample forms
its own cluster, the operator reduces to BoN (no clustering); if all per-sample scores are set to one,
cluster weights collapse to cluster sizes and the operator reduces to Self-Consistency [Wang et al.,
2023] (majority vote). Self-Consistency and Weighted-BoN are therefore the same discrete-MRC
operator at qj ≡ 1 vs. qj =judge-score. Extending the same generalization as above yields Diversity-
MRC-Discrete-N : cycle N samples through the multi-model channel pool, cluster by semantic
equivalence, sum judge scores within clusters, return the top-q member of the winning cluster. On the
matched 8B subset (n=69) Diversity-MRC-Discrete-N achieves q = 0.793 vs. continuous-MRC 0.766
(∆ = +0.027, 26W/29T/14L), with wins in all four categories; canonical single-model Weighted-BoN
/ CISC reduces to the same operator with a single policy and N temperature draws.

Continuous vs. discrete answer spaces. The split between continuous and discrete answer spaces
drives the framework’s two MRC variants: clustering (the discrete path) requires well-defined
semantic equivalence classes, clean on numeric/multiple-choice answers (math, QA, code) but fuzzy
on free-form text; synthesis (the continuous path) trades the clustering LLM call for a combining


                                                 A16
LLM call and avoids the equivalence-class problem at the cost of one extra generation. Empirically,
on our matched 8B split (n=69, hard subset), discrete-MRC dominates continuous-MRC at the
aggregate (qMRC-disc = 0.793 vs. qdMRC = 0.766, ∆ = +0.027, 26W/29T/14L paired), and wins in
every category: +0.043 on QA, +0.029 on creative, +0.022 on reasoning, +0.015 on code. The
ordering flips at 3B on QA (continuous-MRC +0.032) where the voter LLM is too weak to cluster
consistently, confirming that the gap is CSI-limited rather than operator-intrinsic: discrete-MRC
requires a reliable voter to define the equivalence classes, and falls back to the synthesis path when
the voter is noisy.

Why we expect domination at matched budget. The reductions in Table 2 are not loose analogies;
they are hyperparameter specializations. In Diversity MRC the combining weights reduce to majority
vote if we threshold qi at 0.5; in HARQ-IR the critic can be configured to issue unstructured free-form
rewrites (recovering Self-Refine) by removing the JSON critique template and the α-scaled correction
cap. The added mechanism is always a restrictor: it removes degrees of freedom that were empirically
harmful (e.g. over-correction at α = 1, common-mode judge noise without interleaving, parity calls
that duplicate rather than complement the main reasoning). Because each restrictor is applied as
a strict operational change rather than an architectural one, the AGENT C ODEC variant’s expected
quality at matched budget is upper-bounded only by the prior method’s ceiling and typically exceeds
it; the statistically non-zero gap in Table 5 makes this concrete.

G    Standard Benchmark Validation: Hard-Split Construction and Results
To verify that technique rankings from our curated tasks (Sec. 5.3–5.3) are not curation artifacts,
we evaluate on three standard benchmarks, using a hard split of 100 items per benchmark. The
hard split is motivated by our operating-point framing (Corollary 1): channel models saturate near
baseline on easy items, so averaging over the full sets compresses technique differences. We therefore
construct a difficulty-stratified subset per benchmark using published structural proxies rather than
model-conditional filtering, which keeps the split reproducible and model-agnostic:
       • GSM8K-Hard (100). From the 200-item GSM8K subset, we retain the 100 problems with
         the largest number of calculator-annotation steps (≥ 3 steps, range [3, 7]), breaking ties
         by answer magnitude. Step count is the canonical reasoning-length proxy and correlates
         directly with small-model error rates [Cobbe et al., 2021a].
       • MMLU-Hard (100). We restrict to abstract_algebra (100 items), one of the hardest
         MMLU subjects for small open-weight models and a consistent low-scoring category in
         published leaderboards. This isolates multi-step symbolic reasoning from memorization-
         dominated subjects (e.g., anatomy) that our channel models already solve reliably.
       • HumanEval-Hard (100). From the 164 HumanEval problems, we retain the 100 with the
         largest number of doctest examples (ties broken by prompt length). More doctests indicate
         richer specification and tighter edge-case requirements, which are the standard structural
         hardness signal for code synthesis.
The hard splits are shipped with AGENT C ODEC under benchmarks/data_hard/ as drop-in replace-
ments for the originals.

Table 11: Standard-benchmark validation on the Ollama-cloud trio (Nemotron-Nano-3 + Devstral-
Small-2 channels; GLM-5.1 judge; n=100 tasks per split). Baseline is the uncoded single call;
“Best fixed technique” is the highest-mean-quality candidate run on that split; ∆ is the absolute
mean-quality improvement over the per-split baseline.
            Benchmark split    Best fixed technique    Baseline q   Technique q      ∆
            GSM8K-Hard         Diversity-SC-N            0.877         0.969       +0.092
            MMLU-Hard          Fountain                  0.417         0.662       +0.244
            HumanEval-Hard     HARQ-IR                   0.757         0.860       +0.103

The qualitative direction of the technique ranking is preserved across all three hard-benchmark splits
(Figures 12 and 36, Table 11): diversity-style techniques and the HARQ variants remain among
the top performers, the uncoded baseline remains the cheapest but lowest-quality choice, and the
cost-aware semantic-nearest-neighbor router (Table 3) sits on or above the empirical Pareto frontier
on every split. The per-split best fixed technique varies (Diversity-SC-N on GSM8K-Hard, fountain


                                                 A17
on MMLU-Hard, HARQ-IR on HumanEval-Hard; Table 11), tracking the same operating-point
dependence observed on the curated tasks. Per-dataset quality-versus-cost Pareto scatters are in
Figures 8 and 37.

H     Validation experiments
Judge-in-loop confound. Because the same model (Gemma-3 12B on 8B; Phi-3 14B on 14B)
serves as both final scorer and in-loop critic for HARQ/turbo/ACM/SC/BoN, one might worry that
judge-in-loop techniques win by borrowing the 12B model’s capability rather than from the decoding
scheme itself. The oracle-winner distribution on the 8B curated split rebuts the strong version of
this concern: among per-task oracle winners, judge-free-in-decode techniques (baseline, diversity-
EGC, fountain with agreement-based stopping) account for 39.1% of wins (13.0%, 14.5%, 11.6%
respectively), while HARQ-IR and HARQ-CC together account for only 15.9%. Selection-combining
(diversity-SC) is the most frequent winner (18.8%), consistent with a passive-diversity interpretation
rather than a judge-borrowing effect. If the 12B critic were the dominant driver, we would expect
HARQ/turbo/BoN to sweep; they do not. A direct judge-strength ablation (swapping the scorer for a
smaller model while holding techniques fixed) is left to future work due to compute budget.

Synthesis integrity.    Five controlled tests confirm zero synthesizer contamination (Sec. L).

Prediction validation. Four communication-theoretic predictions tested at the cloud-Haiku
operating point (Claude Haiku 4.5 + GPT-5-mini, judge Haiku 4.5; baseline q0 = 0.819):
(P1) MRC ≥ EGC ≥ SC: PASS (observed 0.884 ≥ 0.870 ≥ 0.856, with per-task MRC≻EGC
at 19/17 and EGC≻SC at 28/16, consistent with the high-CSI regime where the binary-checklist
                                          ∗2
judge variance falls below the critical σw   of Prop. 1); (P2) HARQ-IR converges faster than HARQ-
CC: PASS (IR running-max jumps from 0.807 to 0.891 between rounds 1 and 2 and reaches 99% of
its peak by round 3, while CC plateaus more gradually and only reaches 99% of its lower peak 0.878 at
round 4); (P3) diminishing diversity returns: PASS (marginal quality gain per extra channel falls from
+3.74pp (d=1→2, baseline→SC) to +1.38pp per channel (d=2→5, SC→SC-N ), matching the
classical sublinear scaling); (P4) turbo iterations improve quality monotonically: PASS in direction,
FAIL in shape (all-tasks running-max mean climbs 0.808 → 0.869 → 0.882 → 0.898 → 0.902
with the steepest step at iteration 1 and diminishing increments thereafter, but we do not observe a
sharp “waterfall” threshold; per-task gains are diffuse rather than concentrated at a critical iteration).
Overall 3/4 directional passes plus 1/4 in-shape miss; the P1 reversal between cloud-Haiku (PASS)
and 14B-local (FAIL, Sec. 5.3) is itself the empirical signature of the CSI-crossover predicted by
Prop. 1, consistent with our framing of the framework as design-useful and operating-point-aware,
but not yet a quantitative physical theory of LLM channels.

Judge ablation. A controlled judge-strength ablation, varying the judge model while holding
channels and techniques fixed, is the cleanest way to isolate the “in-loop scorer borrows judge
capacity” confound for HARQ-IR, turbo, fountain stopping, ACM, and the best-of-sequence guard.
The same sweep is also the most direct empirical trace of the channel-state-information crossover of
Prop. 1: a weaker judge raises the weighting noise σw , predicted to push the deployment past the
                    ∗2
critical variance σw   and to flip the ordering between MRC
                                                        \ and EGC at fixed channels. Although
we have not run this direct ablation in this study (left for future work), Table 7 varies the judge
across configurations (Gemma-3 12B, Gemma-4 31B, Claude Haiku 4.5, GLM-5.1) only in concert
with channel changes, so the config-to-config preservation of the technique rank order in Table 8
is suggestive of judge-robustness but is confounded with channel-architecture changes and cannot
isolate the judge contribution on its own. The strong-borrowing hypothesis is already partly ruled out
by the oracle-winner decomposition under “Judge-in-loop confound” below (judge-free decoders
still win ≈39% of per-task oracle assignments on the 8B split); a quantitative bound on the in-loop
contribution requires the matched-channel run.

I   Decoding-Threshold Details and Limitations
Per-scale numerical breakdown of the iterative-decoding threshold. (1) Above threshold (14B).
Generator deepseek-r1:14b with critic phi3:14b: turbo +14.2% (p < 10−5 , paired Wilcoxon W =56);


                                                  A18
HARQ-IR +14.0% (p < 10−3 ). The per-iteration all-tasks running-max mean rises monotonically
from 0.726 to 0.853, and the survivor cohort is essentially flat (0.726 → 0.694)—tasks reach a high
ceiling on the first refinement and either early-exit or track it from then on. (2) Near threshold (8B).
Generator llama3.1:8b with critic qwen2.5:7b: turbo +13.9% (0.604 → 0.688); the survivor trajectory
is noisy and slightly declining (0.585, 0.559, 0.556, 0.483, 0.465), while all-tasks running-max still
climbs (0.585 → 0.688). (3) Below threshold (3B). Generator qwen2.5:3b with critic gemma3:4b:
turbo +6.3% absolute gain (0.651 → 0.692) and only marginal over paired Wilcoxon (21 worse / 24
tied / 24 better). The survivor trajectory is net-descending (0.619, 0.514, 0.490, 0.515, 0.560)—the
refinement map is contractive on the wrong side, producing worse outputs than the input on average.
The running-max curve is monotonic (0.619 → 0.692) only because the best-of-sequence guard
(Sec. 4) reverts to earlier iterations when later ones regress. In the communication analog, this is
a decoder operating below the EXIT-chart fixed point—additional iterations degrade rather than
improve the estimate, and only a selection step rescues the output.

Limitations (full discussion). Residual CSI noise: our binary checklist scorer (15 weighted yes/no
criteria, Sec. D) largely resolves the score quantization problem—earlier numeric-rating judges
collapsed 39% of scores to a single value, whereas the checklist produces 215 distinct score levels.
However, borderline criteria still introduce per-evaluation variance (∼0.05–0.10), which can mask
small iterative improvements. Differential scoring for iterative techniques (scoring candidate and
baseline in the same judge call) mitigates this by canceling common-mode judge noise, analogous to
differential decoding. Intrinsic log-probabilities (Remark 3) remain a promising direction for further
reducing CSI noise. Within-task stochastic noise: the 300-task hard-benchmark split (Ollama-cloud
trio) and the 3B local + cloud-judge configuration are evaluated with nrep =3 independent generation
repeats per task, with seed-aware bootstrap propagating within-task generation variance into every
confidence interval (Table 3). The remaining four 69-task curated configurations are single-run
per (task, technique), so means and paired Wilcoxon p-values reported on those configurations
conflate technique effect with within-task stochastic noise (temperature sampling, judge variance);
the codebase supports full repetition (repeat_runs option), and broader repetition is deferred for
compute reasons. Cost overhead: quality scoring adds cost even to easy tasks. Non-stationarity:
model updates degrade channel estimates over time. Dual-role judge: the same model serves as
final scorer and in-loop critic for HARQ/turbo/ACM/SC/BoN. The oracle-winner decomposition
(Sec. H, “Judge-in-loop confound”) shows judge-free-in-decode techniques still win 39% of tasks,
indicating the confound does not determine rankings, but a judge-strength ablation (weaker scorer
with techniques held fixed) remains open. Curated tasks: the 69-task curated split was selected to
exhibit technique differences at the 8–14B operating point (hard/very-hard/extreme tiers); gains on a
random-difficulty split would be smaller. The reproducible hard-split standard benchmarks (Sec. G)
addresses this.

J   Soft-Output Extensions via Token-Level Log-Probabilities
The techniques in the main text treat each agent response as a hard symbol: the combiner sees
only the emitted text and a scalar judge score. Classical receivers, however, benefit enormously
from soft decisions—log-likelihood ratios (LLRs) carry not just the decoded bit but the receiver’s
confidence in it, and every competitive decoder (MAP, BCJR, belief propagation, turbo) is soft-in
soft-out. Autoregressive LLMs expose an analogous quantity for free: the per-token log-probability
log p(yt | y<t , x). We define a response-level confidence
                                            P                      
                                                  N
                              c(y) = exp N1 t=1 log p(yt | y<t , x) ,                          (9)

the geometric mean per-token probability, which plays the role of a channel LLR at the response
level. This section introduces three optional soft variants of our core techniques—S OFT-MRC,
S OFT-F OUNTAIN, and S OFT-ACM—added as additional modes without modifying the hard-decision
pipeline used in Sec. 5.5.

Soft MRC. In classical MRC, branch k is weighted by its instantaneous SNR γk ; our hard variant
uses the judge score as a proxy. SoftPMRC replaces this proxy with the model’s own confidence,
weighting branch k by wk = c(yk )/ j c(yj ) and passing weighted candidates to the synthesizer.
Because synthesis is lossy (Sec. L), we enforce the MRC guarantee SNRMRC ≥ SNRSC by scoring


                                                 A19
the combined output and falling back to selection combining (the single highest-confidence branch)
whenever synthesis scores below the best individual—the direct analog of a CRC check triggering a
retransmission.

Soft Fountain. A rateless decoder stops as soon as it has collected enough reliable symbols. Our
hard fountain variant uses an agreement heuristic; Soft Fountain uses a genuine confidence-based
stopping criterion
                                S = 0.6 · c + 0.4 · agreement,                               (10)
and marks any sample with c(y) < 0.5 cmax as an erasure, mirroring erasure decoding in real fountain
codes rather than quality gating. The ML-style synthesizer only decodes over non-erased samples.

Soft ACM. Hard ACM estimates channel quality via a meta-judgment prompt (“rate this task’s
difficulty”), which we found to be bimodal and to leak uncoded outputs on catastrophic tasks. Soft
ACM instead transmits a short pilot probe (a 2–3 sentence brief answer) and uses c(yprobe ) directly
as the CQI—zero extra judge calls, and grounded in the generator’s own uncertainty rather than
a separate meta-query. The MCS table maps confidence to coding mode with no uncoded floor:
[0.70, 1.0] →FEC r=0.75; [0.50, 0.70] →FEC r=0.5; [0.30, 0.50] →HARQ-IR; [0, 0.30] →MRC
diversity.

Where the analogy tightens. Soft extensions sharpen three gaps we observed in the hard pipeline.
(i) Combining weight fidelity: judge scores are discretized and noisy; c(y) is continuous and generated
by the same process that produced y, matching the SISO recipe more faithfully. (ii) Stopping criteria:
rateless codes are defined by a confidence threshold, not a quality poll. (iii) CQI cost: a pilot probe
costs one short generation, whereas meta-judgment costs a full extra judge call and is an out-of-band
oracle.

Limitations. Soft outputs require backend logprob support: OpenAI, Ollama, and vLLM expose
them, but the Anthropic API does not, so soft techniques raise an explicit error on unsupported
backends rather than silently falling back. The geometric mean in (9) weights all tokens equally
and is blind to which tokens are uncertain (a single wrong fact buried in a confident paragraph can
still dominate correctness); token-level weighting by salience is left to future work. Finally, c(y)
measures the model’s internal confidence, not correctness—it is a calibrated LLR only to the extent
that the generator itself is calibrated.


K    A Larger Design Space

The communication-theoretic view admits a substantially larger design space than the six tech-
niques instantiated in this paper. Concrete mappings we have not built include: (i) interleaving
for decorrelating burst errors in long reasoning chains, treating consecutive reasoning steps as a
coded sequence and permuting the order in which a critic inspects them; (ii) MIMO-style joint
precoding across multiple generator channels, where prompt design plays the role of a precoder
steering each channel into a complementary error mode rather than running them independently;
(iii) LDPC- and polar-style structured FEC over reasoning chains, replacing the uniform repetition
that our FEC implementation currently uses with sparse parity structures that admit message-passing
decoders; (iv) coded computation for verification, exchanging some of the redundant generator calls
in HARQ/turbo for cheaper checksum-style verifier calls in the spirit of coded distributed computing;
(v) OFDM-style subcarrier allocation as principled task decomposition, where independent reasoning
subtasks are assigned to channels under a power-budget constraint analogous to bit-loading; and
(vi) joint source–channel coding for compressing the prompt and its protection scheme together,
rather than the prompt-then-redundancy decomposition implicit in our current pipelines. Each of
these is a well-developed line in classical communications with a natural agentic counterpart that has
not, to our knowledge, been instantiated in either form. The contribution of this paper is to commit
to the channel abstraction concretely enough that those mappings become specifiable rather than
evocative; the limitation is that we have implemented only a small subset and evaluated them on a
constrained compute budget. We hope subsequent work treats this as a research program rather than
a closed set of analogies.


                                                 A20
L     Synthesis integrity validation
For MRC and EGC combining, synthesis contamination is a methodological concern: the synthesizer
model may inject its own knowledge into the combined output rather than faithfully merging the
input responses. Quality gains attributed to combining would then reflect the synthesizer solving the
task independently, invalidating the diversity framework.
We validate synthesis integrity through three controlled experiments using the primary configuration
(DeepSeek-R1 14B + Phi-3 14B channels, Gemma-3 12B judge/synthesizer).

Test 1: Information preservation. Both channel models answer “What is the GCD of 252 and
735?” Both correctly produce 21 via the Euclidean algorithm. The synthesizer preserves the correct
answer and complete reasoning chain without adding computation steps or alternative methods not
present in either input. Result: PASS.

Test 2: No contamination (factual). We construct two deliberately sparse descriptions of Python:
Input A states “created in the early 1990s, interpreted language”; Input B states “high-level, supports
multiple paradigms.” Neither mentions Guido van Rossum, Django, NumPy, indentation-based
syntax, duck typing, or list comprehensions—all facts the synthesizer has seen in training. The
synthesizer output contains zero facts beyond those in the two inputs. Result: PASS.

Test 3: Faithful error preservation. We test whether the synthesizer corrects errors present in
inputs using its own knowledge. Input A states the speed of light was “first measured accurately by
Michelson in 1887”; Input B states relativity was “published in 1915” (the correct year for special
relativity is 1905). The synthesizer faithfully reproduces both claims without correction, confirming
it does not silently fix factual errors using its own knowledge. It also does not add the exact value
299,792,458 m/s (absent from both inputs). Result: PASS.
                                                                                    R3
Test 4: Conflict resolution without independent computation. Input C states 0 x2 dx = 9
                          R3
(correct); Input D states 0 x2 dx = 8 (incorrect). Input C is scored higher (0.80 vs. 0.60). The
synthesizer selects the higher-scored answer (9) without showing its own derivation (e.g., [x3 /3]30 ).
Result: PASS.

Test 5: No prompt leakage. The synthesis prompt uses internal labels ([BEST], [ALT-1]) and
section headers. None of these labels appear in the synthesized output. Result: PASS.
These results confirm that the constrained synthesis prompt (“Your output must contain ONLY
information from the responses above”) combined with low temperature (T = 0.1) effectively
prevents the synthesizer from acting as an independent solver. Quality improvements from MRC/EGC
combining therefore reflect genuine information aggregation across diverse channel outputs, not
synthesizer knowledge injection.

M     Formal Channel-Theoretic Foundations
This appendix provides the information-theoretic arguments underlying the agent channel model
(Sec. 3), stating where established theorems apply and where the agent setting introduces deviations.

M.1    The LLM as a discrete stochastic channel

Lemma 1 (Channel identity). Let Aθ be an autoregressive language model with parameters θ and
temperature T > 0. For any input x ∈ T , the model defines a conditional distribution over output
sequences:
                                               |y|
                                                     p (y | y , x)1/T
                                                   P θ t <t
                                              Y
                               pT (y | x) =                             1/T
                                                                                                    (11)
                                              t=1   v∈V pθ (v | y<t , x)
                                                   P
This is a valid transition probability satisfying y∈V ∗ pT (y | x) = 1 for all x, and thus constitutes a
discrete stochastic channel in the sense of Shannon [Shannon, 1948].


                                                 A21
Proof. The softmax function with temperature scaling produces a valid probability distribution at
each token position: each factor is non-negative and sums to one over V. The product of valid
conditional distributions over a finite sequence (terminated by an end-of-sequence token) yields a
valid joint distribution over V ∗ . The mapping x 7→ pT (· | x) is therefore a well-defined channel
transition probability.

No approximation or limiting argument is involved.

M.2    Conditional entropy and channel noise

For a fixed input x and temperature T , the per-token conditional entropy is:
                                           X
                     HT (Yt | y<t , x) = −      pT (v | y<t , x) log pT (v | y<t , x)             (12)
                                              v∈V

The sequence-level conditional entropy, normalized by output length, is:
                                                     E[|Y |]
                                                1     X
                             HT (Aθ , x) =                   HT (Yt | Y<t , x)                    (13)
                                              E[|Y |] t=1
This quantity is the channel’s intrinsic noise rate in bits per token. It decomposes the factors that
determine channel quality:
      1. Model parameters θ: larger, better-trained models learn sharper conditional distributions
          (lower entropy at each position). Neural scaling laws [Kaplan et al., 2020, Hoffmann et al.,
          2022] show that cross-entropy loss (= our H̄) decreases as a power law L ∝ N −α with
          model parameter count N , providing a quantitative relationship between model size and
          channel noise.
      2. Temperature T : by Remark 1, increasing T flattens the distribution, monotonically in-
          creasing HT . At T = 0, H = 0 (deterministic); at T = ∞, H = log |V| (uniform
          noise).
      3. Task difficulty: for inputs x where the model is uncertain (ambiguous, out-of-distribution,
          or requiring multi-step reasoning), the conditional distribution is naturally more diffuse,
          yielding higher H even at fixed T .
Corollary 1 (Model–difficulty duality). For a given technique with decoding threshold H∗ , the
technique is effective when HT (Aθ , x) < H∗ . Since H depends on both θ (model) and x (task), a
70B model on a hard task and a 3B model on an easy task may occupy the same operating point.
Technique selection should therefore be conditioned on the operating point H, not on model size or
task difficulty independently.

M.3    Mutual information and diversity

For d independent channels Aθ1 , . . . , Aθd (independence holds when models have different architec-
tures and training data, or when temperature variation provides sufficient randomization), the mutual
information satisfies:
                                                           d
                                                           X
                    I(X; Y1 , . . . , Yd ) = I(X; Y1 ) +         I(X; Yi | Y1 , . . . , Yi−1 )    (14)
                                                           i=2

Under independence, I(X; Yi | Y1 , . . . , Yi−1 ) = I(X; Yi ), giving:
                                                              d
                                                              X
                                   I(X; Y1 , . . . , Yd ) =         I(X; Yi )                     (15)
                                                              i=1

Each additional independent channel adds information about X, which is why diversity combining
improves quality. When channels are correlated (shared training data, overlapping architectures),
I(X; Yi | Y1 , . . . , Yi−1 ) < I(X; Yi ), yielding diminishing returns. The effective diversity order
deff < d captures this: it is the number of channels’ worth of independent information in the ensemble.
Turkmen et al. [2026] formalize this for LLM ensembles, showing that MI-based ensemble selection
outperforms greedy accuracy-based selection by accounting for inter-model correlation.


                                                    A22
Empirical branch correlation. Table 12 reports the Pearson correlation r between the two channels’
individual_scores on Diversity-SC, the only technique whose per-branch quality scores are pure
parallel single-shot draws. Aggregate r ranges from 0.17 at 14B local (the configuration closest to
the textbook MRC≥EGC≥SC ordering in Sec. 5.3) to 0.75 on the 3B-Qwen + 3B-Llama pair (the
configuration furthest from it), giving an empirical deff ∈ [1.14, 1.71] from a nominal d=2. On the
headline n=300 hard-benchmark split the aggregate is r=0.665, but Table 13 shows this average
hides a sharp split: r= − 0.03 on the MMLU-QA tasks (near-independent failures) versus r= + 0.73
on HumanEval-code (shared training-data overlap). Configurations and categories with high r are
exactly where EGC’s robustness to CSI noise overtakes MRC  \ in Table 8, the second mechanism
(alongside the CSI-noise variance of Prop. 1) by which the textbook ordering reverses.

M.4    Soft vs. hard channel state information

In classical communications, the combining weights for MRC are derived from the channel’s soft
output. For a Gaussian channel with known noise variance σi2 on branch i, the optimal MRC
weights are wi = 1/σi2 (inverse noise variance weighting) [Proakis and Salehi, 2008]. When
the noise variance must be estimated, any estimation error σ̂i2 ̸= σi2 degrades MRC performance.
Specifically, if the estimation error variance is Var(σ̂i2 ), MRC with estimated weights approaches
EGC performance as Var(σ̂i2 )/σi4 → 1 [Gao et al., 2003].
In our framework, the intrinsic CSI is the log-probability log pθ (y | x), which is a direct measure of
the channel’s confidence. The extrinsic CSI is the LLM judge score. Naive numeric-rating judges are
severely quantized: our initial judge produced only ∼33 unique values across 516 measurements (∼5-
bit resolution), under which the theoretical MRC advantage over EGC vanishes [Simon and Alouini,
2005]. We therefore adopt a sigma-delta scoring design: the judge answers 15 weighted binary criteria
rather than emitting a single number, and the weighted sum recovers a fine-grained score (up to 215
distinct values). Binary decisions are far more reliable for small LLMs than numeric ratings, and the
many-1-bit-measurements-to-high-resolution-output construction mirrors oversampled sigma-delta
ADCs [Norsworthy et al., 1996].


                                                         A23
Practical implication. Log-probability-weighted MRC would use the channel’s own soft output,
avoiding the estimation noise entirely. However, sequence-level log-probability log pθ (y | x) =
P
   t log pθ (yt | y<t , x) has known biases: (i) length bias: shorter sequences tend to have higher log-
probability, favoring terse but incomplete answers; (ii) repetition bias: repeated tokens have artificially
high conditional probability; (iii) calibration: LLM probabilities are not perfectly calibrated [Guo
et al., 2017, Kadavath et al., 2022]. These can be mitigated by length normalization (Eq. (2)) and
temperature calibration, but require empirical validation.

M.5    Iterative decoding threshold

The convergence behavior of iterative decoders (turbo codes, LDPC) is governed by the decoding
threshold [ten Brink, 2001]: a channel noise level H∗ such that:
       • For H < H∗ (good channel): iterative decoding converges, and error probability decreases
         exponentially with iterations.
       • For H > H∗ (bad channel): the decoder cannot converge. Additional iterations may even
         degrade performance due to error propagation.
The threshold depends on the constituent decoder capacity, not on the interleaver design.
In the agent setting, the generator is the constituent decoder and the critic is the interleaver (providing
extrinsic information). Our multi-scale experiments confirm this prediction. At 8B (Llama-3.1 8B,
Qwen-2.5 7B), the marginal gain of HARQ-IR over HARQ-CC nearly vanishes (HARQ-CC +22.7%,
HARQ-IR +22.8%, turbo +13.9% in Table 8; the HARQ-IR-over-HARQ-CC gap is 0.1 pp, vs.
2.3 pp at 14B), and the turbo survivor cohort is noisy with no monotone trend (0.585 → 0.559 →
0.556 → 0.483 → 0.465; Fig. 18, Fig. 3 center)—consistent with near-threshold operation, where
extrinsic critique fails to propagate into improved iterates. At 14B (DeepSeek-R1 14B, Phi-3 14B),
iterative gains are more sustained: HARQ-IR +14.0% exceeds HARQ-CC +11.7% by 2.3 pp, turbo
reaches +14.2%, and the all-tasks running-max climbs monotonically from 0.726 to 0.853 across
five iterations (Fig. 2, Fig. 14), suggesting these models cross into the waterfall region for a larger
fraction of tasks. The cloud configuration (Claude Haiku 4.5, GPT-5-mini, q0 = 0.819) shows the
clearest above-threshold behavior: HARQ-IR +10.8% and turbo +10.2% both produce monotonic
improvement, with HARQ-IR exceeding HARQ-CC by 3.4 pp (Table 8; HARQ and turbo trajectories
in Fig. 29).
This is consistent with the threshold being a property of the generator’s conditional entropy rather than
the critique mechanism. The comparison across 8B → 14B → cloud is consistent with a transition
from noise-amplification (below threshold) to stable iterative improvement (above threshold); we
stop short of claiming a full waterfall, since neither the number of iterations nor the number of tasks
in our study is sufficient to resolve the sharp transition characteristic of bit-error-rate waterfalls.

A fixed-point characterization of the threshold. The threshold can be made precise by modeling
the per-iteration map on quality directly. Let Rx : Y → Y be the refinement operator (generator +
critic + extrinsic-information exchange) for prompt x, and define the quality-update map
                                                                  
                               f (q0 ) := E q Rx (Y ) q(Y ) = q0 ,
                 (k)
  qk := f (q0 ) the k-fold deterministic iterate of expected quality, and Qk :=
with
E max0≤j≤k q(Yj ) the expected quality under the best-of-sequence guard (Sec. O.1). The guarded
delivered sequence is monotone independently of the dynamics of f :
Remark 4 (Best-of-sequence monotonicity). For every refinement trajectory (Y0 , Y1 , . . . ), the
running maximum max0≤j≤k q(Yj ) is non-decreasing in k pathwise; taking expectations gives
Qk ≥ Qk−1 ≥ · · · ≥ q0 for all k ≥ 1.

This is a one-line consequence of monotonicity of the maximum over a growing index set; it makes
no use of the structure of f , and we use it throughout to read the empirical running-max curves of
Figures 2 and 3 as predictions about the delivered output even when the unguarded iterate qk behaves
badly. The substantive content of the threshold therefore lies in the local stability of the high-quality
fixed point of f .
Proposition 2 (Refinement contraction and the iterative-decoding threshold). Suppose f : [0, 1] →
[0, 1] is continuously differentiable with at least one fixed point q ∞ ∈ (0, 1].


                                                  A24
(i) Above threshold. If |f ′ (q ∞ )| < 1, then for every κ ∈ |f ′ (q ∞ )|, 1 there exists a neighborhood
                                                                            

    U ∋ q ∞ with f (U ) ⊆ U such that
                                |f (q) − q ∞ | ≤ κ |q − q ∞ |       for all q ∈ U,
    and consequently, for every q0 ∈ U ,
                                                                     k→∞
                                    |qk − q ∞ | ≤ κk |q0 − q ∞ | −−−−→ 0.
     Identifying qk with the conditional mean E[q(Yk ) | q0 ] (the EXIT-recursion reading of f ), this
     gives lim inf k Qk ≥ q ∞ ; the squeeze qk ≤ Qk ≤ q ∞ forces Qk → q ∞ whenever q(Yj ) ≤ q ∞
     almost surely along the trajectory (in particular when q ∞ = 1).
(ii) Below threshold. If, in addition, f is non-decreasing on [0, 1], |f ′ (q ∞ )| > 1, and f admits a
     second stable fixed point q − < q ∞ with |f ′ (q − )| < 1 and no other fixed point in [q − , q ∞ ], then
     for every q0 ∈ (q − , q ∞ ) the unguarded iterate satisfies qk ↘ q − , while the guarded sequence still
     satisfies Qk ≥ q0 for all k by Remark 4.

 Proof. (i) Local contraction. Fix κ ∈ (|f ′ (q ∞ )|, 1). Continuity of f ′ at q ∞ gives δ > 0 such that
 |f ′ (q)| ≤ κ for all q ∈ U := (q ∞ − δ, q ∞ + δ) ∩ [0, 1]. The set U is convex and contains q ∞ , so for
 any q ∈ U the segment from q to q ∞ lies in U and the mean-value theorem yields some ξ ∈ U with
 f (q) − q ∞ = f (q) − f (q ∞ ) = f ′ (ξ) (q − q ∞ ), hence
                                     |f (q) − q ∞ | ≤ κ |q − q ∞ | < δ.
 Combined with f (q) ∈ [0, 1], this gives f (q) ∈ U , so f (U ) ⊆ U and the iteration qk+1 = f (qk )
 remains in U once q0 ∈ U . Induction on k now yields |qk − q ∞ | ≤ κ |qk−1 − q ∞ | ≤ · · · ≤
 κk |q0 − q ∞ |, and κ < 1 forces qk → q ∞ .
 Guarded sequence. Pathwise, max0≤j≤k q(Yj ) ≥ q(Yk ), so under the mean-field reading E[q(Yk )] =
 qk we have Qk ≥ qk , hence lim inf k Qk ≥ q ∞ . The reverse bound Qk ≤ q ∞ holds whenever
 q(Yj ) ≤ q ∞ almost surely along the trajectory, and the two together give Qk → q ∞ .
 (ii) Set g(q) := f (q)−q. Then g(q − ) = g(q ∞ ) = 0 and, since |f ′ (q − )| < 1, g ′ (q − ) = f ′ (q − )−1 <
 0; together with the assumption that g has no other zero in [q − , q ∞ ], this fixes the sign of g as negative
 on the open interval, i.e. f (q) < q on (q − , q ∞ ). Monotonicity of f gives f (q) ≥ f (q − ) = q − , so f
 maps (q − , q ∞ ) into [q − , q ∞ ) and qk is well-defined and non-increasing there. Bounded below by
 q − , qk converges by monotone convergence and continuity to a fixed point of f in [q − , q ∞ ), which
 can only be q − . The guard inequality is Remark 4.

 Attribution. The mathematical content of Prop. 2 is a specialization of the classical local-stability
 theory of one-dimensional smooth maps to the present setting (see, e.g., Hirsch et al., 2013, Ch. 9;
 the contraction case is the one-dimensional Banach–Picard corollary, and the bistable case is the
 standard saddle-node picture). The interpretation of the dichotomy |f ′ (q ∞ )| ≶ 1 as a decoding
 threshold is the EXIT-chart / density-evolution analysis of ten Brink [2001] and Richardson and
 Urbanke [2001], in which the threshold is the channel parameter at which the high-quality fixed
 point of a one-dimensional recursion loses local stability. Our contribution here is the modeling step,
 identifying the LLM refinement operator Rx as the constituent decoder, the critique channel as the
 extrinsic-information exchange, and the quality-update map f (q0 ) = E[q(Rx (Y )) | q(Y ) = q0 ] as
 the EXIT recursion; we restate the underlying dynamical-systems result here so that the threshold
 prediction in Fig. 3 can be read off the same |f ′ | condition that controls turbo and LDPC convergence.
 Interpretation. The condition |f ′ (q ∞ )| < 1 at the high-quality fixed point is the agent analog of
 operating above the iterative-decoding threshold of a turbo code [ten Brink, 2001]: refinement is
 locally contractive toward q ∞ and EXIT-style trajectories converge to it. When this condition fails
 (the upper fixed point becomes unstable), trajectories collapse toward a lower-quality basin, and only
 the best-of-sequence guard prevents the delivered output from following them down. Fig. 3 is the
 empirical signature of this transition: the survivor-cohort mean (an empirical proxy for qk ) descends
 at 3B, is flat-noisy at 8B, and stays at the upper fixed point at 14B, while the running-max curve (an
 empirical proxy for Qk ) is monotone in all three panels, exactly the asymmetry that Remark 4 and
 part (ii) of Prop. 2 predict.
 What the theorem does and does not give. It does not estimate |f ′ (q ∞ )| for any concrete genera-
 tor/critic pair (this would require many independent refinement trajectories per quality bin, beyond our


                                                     A25
compute budget). It gives a structural prediction — that the empirical transition between 3B/8B/14B
should look like a transition between subcritical, critical, and supercritical iteration, and that part (i)
decouples the deliverable quality from the unguarded dynamics — and that prediction matches the
observed pattern.

M.6    Bounds on agent channel capacity

While the exact capacity C = maxp(x) I(X; Y ) is intractable for LLM channels, useful bounds exist.
Upper bound. For any output distribution, I(X; Y ) ≤ H(Y ) ≤ E[|Y |]·log |V|. A tighter bound uses
the observation that LLM outputs occupy a tiny fraction of V ∗ : natural language has approximately 1–
1.5 bits/character of entropy [Shannon, 1948, Cover and Thomas, 2006], giving H(Y ) ≈ 1.2 · E[|Y |]
bits.
Lower bound. Any achievable quality Q∗ (C) under cost budget C provides a lower bound, since
the technique configuration that achieves Q∗ is a specific (suboptimal) coding scheme. Our ACM
experiments (Sec. 5.4) thus provide constructive lower bounds.
Rate interpretation. In our setting, the “rate” is the semantic information density: how many bits
of task-relevant content are transmitted per token of model output. FEC at code rate r trades this
density for reliability: lower r adds more parity tokens, reducing information density but increasing
the fraction of tasks solved correctly. This directly parallels the rate–reliability tradeoff in Shannon’s
coding theorem.

                                                    A26
O     Detailed implementation of each technique
This appendix documents the concrete implementation of every technique benchmarked in this paper,
at the level of detail required for an independent reimplementation. All techniques are built from
two shared primitives: the agent channel A and the quality scorer q. Before describing individual
techniques we fix notation and document these primitives, since the guards and guarantees of each
technique rely on them.

O.1   Shared primitives

Agent channel A. A channel is a callable A : prompt × temperature → output that wraps an
LLM endpoint (OpenAI-compatible, Anthropic native, or a local Ollama/vLLM server). A call
returns an AgentOutput record with fields text, model, temperature, token_count, cost_usd,
latency_s, and—optionally—token_logprobs and mean_logprob when the backend supports
them. Reasoning-model thinking blocks (<think>...</think> for DeepSeek-R1 / Qwen3; An-
thropic ThinkingBlock for Claude) are stripped from the visible text before downstream use, but
the thinking tokens remain in the cost accounting. Whenever possible we disable thinking.

Quality scorer q. The scorer is a binary checklist judge: given (prompt, candidate, optional
reference) it asks a dedicated LLM (the judge, typically gemma3:12b locally or claude-haiku-4-5 in
the cloud) to evaluate 15 weighted yes/no criteria and returns the weighted true-rate in [0, 1]. Criteria
are fixed per task type (with / without reference) and weights sum to 1; a “good but not perfect”
answer is calibrated to pass 10–12 of 15 checks. This produces 215 = 32,768 possible score values,
avoiding the severe quantization of numeric-rating judges (Sec. D). On tasks with objective checks,
the final score is a 0.6 · objective + 0.4 · checklist blend.
The scorer additionally exposes a differential variant q∆ : to compare a candidate against a baseline
with known score q0 , the judge scores both in the same call pattern, and the candidate score is reported
as q0 + (qcand − qbase-rescore ), clamped to [0, 1]. This cancels common-mode judge noise and is used
by every technique that refines a previous answer (HARQ-IR, turbo, FEC post-decode, diversity
synthesis).

Output contract. Every technique returns a ReliabilityRun record containing: task_id,
technique, individual_outputs (all generator calls), overhead_outputs (synthe-
sizer/critic/decoder/judge calls not directly part of the answer), combined_output (the final answer
text), final_quality (the judge score of the combined output), and rounds (iterations used).
Cost accounting sums all cost_usd fields across individual_outputs ∪ overhead_outputs
∪ judge calls. Techniques that synthesize or refine (diversity MRC/EGC, fountain, HARQ,
turbo, FEC) implement a best-of-sequence guard: if the post-synthesis score is lower than the best
individual score seen, the final output reverts to the best individual output. This guard is what makes
the running-max curves in Fig. 3 monotonic regardless of refinement quality.

O.2   Baseline (uncoded transmission)

A single channel call with the task prompt. No parity, no diversity, no feedback: Y ← A(X),
q0 ← q(X, Y ), return Y with score q0 . This is the “uncoded” reference point; all gains are re-
ported relative to it after paired matching on task_id (and, when repeat_runs>1, on (task_id,
repeat_idx)).

O.3   Diversity ensemble (SC / MRC / EGC)

Analog. Multipath diversity. Generate d noisy copies and combine: SC picks the best, MRC
weights by quality, EGC weights equally.

Algorithm. Let A1 , . . . , Ad be channels (spatial diversity: different model families; frequency
diversity: different prompt variants of the same question; time diversity: different temperature draws
of the same model). Generate Yi ← Ai (X) and score all in parallel: qi ← q(X, Yi ).
• SC: return Yi∗ where i∗ = arg maxi qi . No synthesizer call.


                                                  A27
• MRC / EGC: pass the full list {(Yi , qi , modeli )} to a synthesizer LLM As (the judge model, which
  is strictly stronger than the channel models and has already seen the candidates during scoring).
The MRC synthesis prompt marks the top-scoring response as [BEST] and others as [ALT-i] with
their quality scores, and instructs: start from [BEST]; add only details from [ALT-i] that do not
contradict [BEST]; prefer higher-quality responses on conflicts; do not introduce external facts. The
EGC prompt is similar but presents all responses as “equally weighted” and uses majority reasoning
for conflicts. Synthesis is called with temperature 0.1 (MRC) or 0.2 (EGC).


Guards. (i) MRC dominance fast path. If all non-best scores satisfy qi < 0.5 · qmax , synthesis
is skipped and SC is returned—analogous to one branch dominating in AWGN MRC. (ii) Best-of-
sequence. The synthesis output is rescored via q∆ against the best individual. If the result does
not improve over the best individual, SC output is returned instead. This enforces the combining
guarantee MRC ≥ SC empirically, even when the synthesizer has regressions. (iii) Identity detection.
If the synthesizer returns the best individual text verbatim, no rescore is performed (the best-individual
score is used directly, saving a judge call).


Defaults. d = 2 spatial branches (two different model families); prompt variants = 1 (default
wording only); temperature spread unused in the main tables (all branches at each model’s configured
default temperature).


Wider-pool variants: diversity_sc_N and diversity_mrc_discrete_N. The two wider-
pool operators referenced in Table 6 share the same multi-model sampling rule and differ only in
the combiner. Sampling: both draw N candidates by cycling through the configured channel pool in
round-robin order—sample i is generated by channel index i mod |{A1 , . . . , Ad }| at temperature
0.7, so an N =5 run with d=2 channels uses {A1 , A2 , A1 , A2 , A1 }. Each candidate is independently
scored by the judge to obtain qi . diversity_sc_N: return the argmax-qi sample. No synthesizer
or voter call; total cost is N generator calls +N judge calls. diversity_mrc_discrete_N: cluster
the N samples into semantic equivalence classes via one extra voter LLM call (defaults to A1 ) at
temperature 0.0; the voter receives the task plus all N samples and returns a JSON array [ℓ1 , . . . , ℓN ]
of cluster IDs. We sum qi within each cluster, pick the cluster with the largest sum, and return
its top-qi member as the combined output. Failure handling: if voter output cannot be parsed as a
length-N JSON integer array, every sample is assigned to its own cluster and the operator degenerates
to diversity_sc_N on that task. Total cost is N generator calls +N judge calls +1 voter call; the
voter call is recorded in the run’s overhead bucket. Defaults. N = 5; voter = A1 (the first configured
channel). The operator’s continuous-MRC counterpart (diversity_mrc with d = N ) trades the
voter call for an LLM synthesis call, so the per-task call counts are equal up to that one substitution;
the empirical comparison in Sec. 5.2 attributes the discrete-MRC win at 8B and its 3B-QA reversal to
voter-LLM clustering quality rather than to a difference in budget.


Discrete-MRC vs. CISC: orthogonal generalizations. CISC [Taubenfeld et al., 2025] and
diversity_mrc_discrete_N both apply discrete-MRC (cluster, sum CSI per cluster, return
the top-CSI member of the winning cluster) but specialize different axes of the same underly-
ing operator. CISC fixes the pool to a single policy (one model, N temperature draws) and
feeds intrinsic token-logprob confidence as the per-sample weight, eliminating the judge call.
diversity_mrc_discrete_N fixes the CSI source to the judge score and generalizes the pool
to a multi-model channel set sampled in round-robin. Neither subsumes the other: the strict superset
is Diversity-MRC-Discrete-N -Soft—multi-model pool with logprob CSI—which collapses to CISC
at |channels|=1 and to diversity_mrc_discrete_N at judge-CSI. We implement Diversity-MRC-
Discrete-N -Soft as diversity_mrc_discrete_N_soft in Sec. O.10 and report it on the local
Ollama configurations only; the cloud configuration uses Anthropic Haiku, which does not expose
logprobs, so that channel must be dropped before this technique can run. We flag a residual concern:
logprobs from different model families are not on the same scale (different vocabularies, different cali-
bration), so summing ci across models inside a cluster is a heuristic rather than a calibrated weighting.
CISC sidesteps this because all N samples come from one policy. The empirical question—whether
multi-model pool gains compose additively with intrinsic-CSI gains, despite cross-model logprob
incomparability—is what this technique is meant to answer.


                                                   A28
O.4   Hybrid ARQ – Chase Combining (HARQ-CC)

Analog.    Chase combining: same codeword retransmitted, receiver soft-combines all copies.

Algorithm. Up to K = 5 retransmissions of the same prompt to the same channel (with independent
stochastic sampling each call). After each retransmission k, if qk ≥ τ = 0.85, stop and return Yk .
Otherwise, at the end, pass all {Y1 , . . . , YK } to a chase-combining synthesizer: a prompt that shows
every attempt with its quality score and instructs to (1) detect consensus (high-confidence signal),
(2) harvest unique details from individual attempts, (3) resolve conflicts in favor of higher-quality
attempts. The synthesized output is rescored via q∆ against the best individual, with the best-of-
sequence guard applied.

Defaults. K = 5, τ = 0.85, synthesizer = the critic channel (same model family as the generator,
communication-faithful) at temperature 0.3.

O.5   Hybrid ARQ – Incremental Redundancy (HARQ-IR)

Analog. Incremental redundancy: each retransmission carries new parity bits. In our analog, the
“new parity” is structured critic feedback that points to specific errors in the previous attempt.

Algorithm. Let Ag be the generator channel and Ac be the critic channel (default: Ac = Ag ,
matching real HARQ decoders).
1. Round 1. Y1 ← Ag (X); q1 ← q(X, Y1 ) (absolute scoring). Initialize Y ⋆ ← Y1 , q ⋆ ← q1 , score
   history S ← [q1 ], correction buffer C ← ∅. If q1 ≥ τ = 0.85, return.
2. Rounds k = 2, . . . , K = 5.
    (a) Critic pass. The critic is shown the task, the current best Y ⋆ , the current score q ⋆ , the
         optional reference, and a deduplication list of already-addressed quoted issues from C. It
         is instructed to return a JSON array of issues, each a record {quote, type∈ {factual_error,
         missing_content, reasoning_gap, unclear}, correction or detail, severity∈ {critical,
         major, minor}}. Temperature 0.2.
    (b) Parse. A multi-stage JSON parser (raw → stripped-backtick → fenced-code-block → inline-
         array) tolerates weak critics. Explicit [] or PASS text yields an empty issue list. Parse failures
         degrade to a single unstructured issue with the raw text.
    (c) Early-stop checks (only when early_exit=True). If the issue list is empty and q ⋆ ≥ 0.9τ ,
         break (genuine convergence). If the score history has plateaued over a window of 2 with
         spread < 0.015, break (EXIT-chart flatness).
    (d) Generator pass. If the issue list is structured (at least one item has a quote), the refine-
         ment prompt becomes a correction list: for each issue, show the quoted text plus the
         requested fix / insertion, and explicitly instruct the generator to preserve every other part
         of the answer verbatim. If the issues are unstructured, fall back to a full-rewrite prompt.
         Yk ← Ag (refine-prompt); qk ← q∆ (X, Yk , Y ⋆ , q ⋆ ) (differential scoring against the current
         best).
    (e) Accept / reject. If qk ≥ q ⋆ , set Y ⋆ ← Yk , q ⋆ ← qk , and append applied corrections to C.
         Else, keep Y ⋆ unchanged (regression protection) and do not enlarge C (so the same issues
         remain available to the critic next round).
     (f) Score-history update. S ← S ∪ {qk }. If q ⋆ ≥ τ , break.
Return Y ⋆ with score q ⋆ .

Communication-faithful defaults. early_exit=False (all K rounds run, matching real decoders);
Ac = Ag (same-complexity decoder).

Key design choices. (i) Correction-based refinement (non-destructive accumulation): the generator
is prompted to apply specific corrections while preserving correct content, not to rewrite the answer
from scratch. This is the agent analog of the soft-buffer accumulation in real HARQ-IR—the
previous answer is the “received signal” and corrections are accumulated parity bits. (ii) Structured
JSON critique forces the critic to quote specific text and tag severity, enabling deduplication across
rounds. Vague feedback is the analog of retransmitting identical parity—zero incremental information.


                                                  A29
(iii) Differential scoring per round reduces judge noise: scoring candidate and baseline in the same
judge call cancels common-mode variance.

O.6   Turbo decoder

Analog. Two SISO decoders exchanging extrinsic information through an interleaver; each iteration
one decoder updates its belief using the other’s extrinsic LLRs, with a damping factor α ∈ (0, 1] to
prevent oscillation [Vogt and Finger, 2000].

Algorithm. Let Ag be the generator, Ac the critic (default: Ac = Ag , “same” mode), q the judge.
Maintain best Y ⋆ , best score q ⋆ , correction buffer C, score history S, current damping α ← α0 = 0.5,
and consecutive-regression counter r.
1. Iteration 0. Y0 ← Ag (X); q0 ← q(X, Y0 ); initialize. If q0 ≥ τ = 0.9, return.
2. Iterations k = 1, . . . , K − 1 = 5 (total max iterations including iter 0 is 6, but the default
   configuration caps at K = 5):
    (a) Interleaver. Select a lens from {correctness, completeness, reasoning, clarity}
         by index k mod 4. The lens is appended to the critic’s system instruction (“pay special
         attention to X this round, but flag any problem”). This decorrelates critic observations across
         iterations—the agent analog of the bit-interleaver in real turbo codes. Without the interleaver
         the critic tends to produce correlated feedback (same issues flagged every round), reducing
         extrinsic information to near-zero.
    (b) Critic pass. Same structured-JSON critique as HARQ-IR, but with the lens hint and the
         dedup list of already-applied quoted issues from C.
    (c) Extrinsic scaling (the α step). Apply three filters in sequence to the critic’s issue list:
           • Severity floor. Drop issues below severity_floor=major (i.e. only critical and
             major survive). Minor issues on already-polished answers are the critic fabricating
             problems—agent analog of LLR magnitude thresholding.
           • α scaling. Keep ⌈n · α⌉ issues (at least one, if any remain), sorted by severity ascending
             (most severe first). This is the direct agent analog of extrinsic-LLR scaling in turbo
             decoders: limit how aggressively each iteration can correct.
           • Hard cap. max_corrections_per_round= 2 issues total (LLR clipping).
    (d) Generator pass. Apply the ≤ 2 surviving corrections via the same correction-list prompt as
         HARQ-IR (preserve-except-corrections contract). Score via q∆ against Y ⋆ . Append qk to S.
    (e) Accept / reject with adaptive damping. If qk ≥ q ⋆ : accept, append applied corrections to C,
         reset r ← 0, and relax α ← min(α0 , 1.2α). Else: reject (regression protection), increment r,
         and damp α ← max(0.1, 0.5α).
     (f) Divergence break. If r ≥ 2 (two consecutive regressions), break. The EXIT trajectory is
         diverging and further iterations will consume tokens without recovery. The best-of-sequence
         guard ensures Y ⋆ is still the best seen.
    (g) Convergence break. If q ⋆ ≥ τ , break.
Return Y ⋆ with score q ⋆ .

Key design choices. (i) Same-complexity decoder (Ac = Ag ) matches real turbo codes, where
both component decoders operate on the same trellis. Allowing a stronger critic is supported
(critic_model="judge") but breaks the analogy. (ii) Communication-faithful fixed iterations:
early_exit=False runs all K rounds. The divergence break on r ≥ 2 is activity-dependent;
the plateau-detection break is gated by early_exit=True. (iii) Interleaver lens rotation is what
distinguishes turbo from HARQ-IR in our implementation. With the same critic and no interleaver,
the two techniques become identical.

Why α0 = 0.5, severity floor = major. Empirical analysis on 14B models showed the first
refinement round at α = 0.7 produced the largest regression (hard-task mean 0.627 → 0.592),
consistent with over-correction even at moderate damping. Lowering α0 to 0.5 and gating minor
issues eliminated the first-round regression without harming overall gain. At 3B/4B (Fig. 3), even
α0 = 0.5 is insufficient—the refinement map itself is contractive on the wrong side, and the best-of-
sequence guard does all the work.


                                                 A30
O.7   Fountain (Rateless) Decoder

Analog. LT / Raptor codes [Shokrollahi, 2006]: generate an unbounded stream of encoded symbols
and stop when the receiver can decode. No fixed code rate.


Algorithm.    Parameters: Nmax = 10, Nmin = 2, confidence threshold γ = 0.85.
1. For n = 1, . . . , Nmax , round-robin across channels A1+(n−1) mod d ; temperature perturbed by
   0.5 + 0.1(n mod 5) for stochastic encoding. Yn ← A(X, temp); qn ← q(X, Yn ).
2. After each sample, if n ≥ Nmin , compute confidence γn = 0.6 · q + 0.4 · agreement, where q is
   the mean score so far and agreement = 1 − (max − min) over the top-⌈n/2⌉ scores. If γn ≥ γ,
   stop.
3. ML decode. Sort samples by quality descending. If the best sample dominates the runner-up by
   > 0.20, return it directly (ML-decode fast path, no synthesis). Else, apply erasure marking: drop
   samples whose score is > 0.10 below
                                    P the best. The surviving set is passed to a weighted-synthesis
   prompt with weights wi = qi / qj and explicit instructions “trust the higher-weighted sample
   unconditionally on conflicts; add details from lower-weighted samples only when they do not
   contradict”. The synthesized output is scored independently and subjected to the best-of-sequence
   guard.


Key design choices. Erasure marking (drop samples > 0.10 below best) is what distinguishes this
from naive ensembling: earlier versions that merged all top-half samples leaked wrong facts from
weak samples into the answer (measured −0.024 vs best individual across 69 tasks). The ML decoder
now either picks the single best (fast path) or synthesizes only from a tight quality band.


O.8   Forward Error Correction (FEC)

Analog. Structured redundancy: generate systematic bits plus parity sections. Code rate r = k/n
controls the redundancy level.


Algorithm. The systematic bits are the main answer: Y0 ← A(X), q0 ← q(X, Y0 ). Each parity
section is a separate LLM call with the main answer in context but a distinct instruction:
• reasoning: re-derive the answer from scratch via step-by-step reasoning; flag any discrepancy
  with the main answer.
• verification: check each claim in the main answer for correctness, logical validity, complete-
  ness, and internal consistency; state each issue with a quote, a reason, and a correction.
• alternative: solve the task using a different approach / strategy without reference to the main
  answer. Cross-check.
• confidence: rate each claim HIGH / MEDIUM / LOW confidence; flag LOW items as potential
  errors.
Code rates map to parity-section lists: r = 1.0 7→ [] (uncoded), r = 0.75 7→ [reasoning], r =
0.50 7→ [reasoning, verification], r = 0.33 7→ [reasoning, verification, alternative],
r = 0.25 7→ all four. The decoder sees the main answer and all parity sections as separate numbered
blocks and runs a syndrome-decoding prompt: for each present parity section, include a bullet
describing what to cross-check (“does step-by-step reach the same conclusion?”, “did verification
find errors?”, “does the independent solution agree?”, “are LOW-confidence items actually wrong?”);
detect inconsistencies; correct; output the final answer. Decoder temperature 0.2. The decoded output
is rescored via q∆ against Y0 with the best-of-sequence guard.


Key design choice. Each parity section is a separate LLM call, not a single prompt that asks for
everything. Cramming multiple checks into one call produces attention dilution and correlated “parity”
(all sections restating the main answer). Separate calls give each check the model’s full attention and
produce genuinely independent redundancy, matching how real FEC computes each parity symbol
independently.


                                                 A31
O.9    Adaptive Coding & Modulation (ACM)

Analog. LTE / 5G ACM: a modulation-and-coding scheme (MCS) table is indexed by measured
channel quality (CQI); good channels get high-rate / low-protection MCS, bad channels get low-rate /
high-protection MCS.

Algorithm. Profiles (MCS levels) are specified as a list of {name, difficulty_range, model,
technique, code_rate (for FEC), num_branches (for diversity), max_rounds (for HARQ),
cost_multiplier}.
1. Difficulty estimation (CQI). The pilot probe: a short 2–3 sentence generation on the task, requested
   with token-level log-probabilities. The probe’s mean logprob ℓ is the CQI; difficulty is d =
   1 − exp(ℓ), clamped to [0, 1]. This is a direct analog of channel estimation from pilot symbols
   in physical-layer ACM. When the backend does not expose logprobs (e.g., Anthropic channels),
   ACM falls back to an LLM self-rating call (“rate this task’s difficulty [0, 1]” at T =0.1). The probe
   is billed as overhead.
2. Profile selection. Find the first MCS whose difficulty_range contains the estimated difficulty;
   fall through to the maximum-protection MCS on miss.
3. Execute. Instantiate the selected technique (HARQ-IR / turbo / fountain / diversity-MRC / diversity-
   EGC / FEC / uncoded) with the profile’s parameters, using the profile’s model (if available in the
   channel pool; else the first channel).
Routing tables are calibrated per model tier. The 3B table (qwen2.5:3b + gemma3:4b) routes
MCS-0/1/2 to fountain with n=2 spatial branches and MCS-3 to diversity-EGC; fountain dom-
inates every bucket at this scale. The 14B table (deepseek-r1:14b + phi3:14b) uses HARQ-IR
(max_rounds=2→3) for MCS-0/1 and turbo (max_rounds=4→6) for MCS-2/3/4, reflecting the
14B leaderboard where iterative refinement beats spatial diversity. The Haiku + GPT-5-mini table
routes MCS-0/1/2 to HARQ-IR, MCS-3 to diversity-MRC, and MCS-4 to turbo.

O.10    Soft-Output Variants

When the backend exposes token-level log-probabilities (Ollama, vLLM, OpenAI), three techniques
have soft-decision counterparts that replace judge-based weights with intrinsic model confidence
c = exp(ℓ) where ℓ is the mean token log-probability of the generation. Anthropic models do not
currently expose logprobs; these modes fail fast rather than silently degrade.
                                                                                               P
SoftDiversityMRC. Identical structure to MRC, but the combining weights are wi = ci / j cj
                 P
rather than qi / j qj . Eliminates the judge call that would otherwise be needed to weight branches,
at the cost of a weaker signal (logprob reflects the model’s own uncertainty, not output correctness).
The SC fallback on synthesis regression is unchanged.

SoftFountainDecoder. Replaces the quality-based confidence estimator with a soft one: γn =
0.6 · c + 0.4 · (1 − spread(c)), and erasure-marks samples with ci < 0.5cmax . Low-logprob samples
are dropped from synthesis, mirroring how real fountain decoders discard erasure-flagged symbols.

SoftACMRouter. Replaces the explicit difficulty-estimation LLM call with a pilot probe: a short
generation (2–3 sentences) on the task, requested with logprobs. The probe’s mean logprob is the CQI:
high logprob → easy channel → light protection; low logprob → hard channel → heavy protection.
Profiles are keyed by confidence range rather than difficulty range.

O.11    Where Each Technique Can Fail (and Why That Is a Feature)

Because all techniques share the best-of-sequence guard, their final quality can never drop below the
best individual call. But their per-iteration or per-combining-step dynamics differ sharply across the
operating regime, and these differences are what the framework predicts:
• Diversity (SC/MRC/EGC) degrades only if branches are strongly correlated (shared training data),
  reducing the effective diversity order. The MRC ≥ EGC prediction assumes near-perfect CSI; in
  our data (Sec. 5.3, Sec. C) CSI is noisy enough on occasions that EGC ≥ MRC reverses.


                                                 A32
• HARQ-CC fails when all attempts share the same failure mode; synthesis cannot correct errors that
  every attempt makes.
• HARQ-IR / Turbo fail below the iterative-decoding threshold: the refinement map is expansive
  rather than contractive, and extra iterations inject noise. The α damping plus divergence break plus
  best-of-sequence guard contain the damage but do not create gain. Fig. 3 is the visualization of this
  failure mode at 3B.
• Fountain can over-generate on adversarial tasks where the confidence metric is miscalibrated (many
  high-quality-looking but wrong samples), reaching Nmax without stopping; the synthesized output
  is still best-of-sequence-guarded, so cost is wasted but quality is not harmed.
• FEC fails when the parity sections themselves share the main answer’s errors (the critic/verifier is
  misled by the main answer’s framing). Separate LLM calls with different instructions (verification
  vs. independent re-derivation) mitigate but do not eliminate this.
• ACM fails when the difficulty estimator is systematically miscalibrated, routing hard tasks to
  MCS-0 or easy tasks to MCS-4. Soft ACM replaces the meta-judgment with a direct logprob
  measurement, reducing this risk at the cost of requiring logprob support.


P     Per-configuration figure gallery

The same plotting pipeline (agentcodec.plots.plot_all_from_cache) is applied to every chan-
nel configuration of Table 7, producing a per-configuration set of plots from the single-call baseline
cache and the per-technique caches. We reproduce the five most informative plots per configuration
here and consolidate the remaining figures (heatmaps, quality distributions, FEC rate–distortion,
matched-budget bars) on the project release. Throughout this gallery, “ρ” denotes per-task cost over-
head relative to the uncoded baseline (Eq. (7)); all error bars are 95% bootstrap confidence intervals;
all significance stars are paired Wilcoxon signed-rank vs. baseline (∗ p<0.05, ∗∗ p<0.01, ∗∗∗ p<10−3 ).
Communication-theoretic technique names referenced in legends are: D IVERSITY-SC, D IVERSITY-
MRC, D IVERSITY-EGC (selection / maximal-ratio / equal-gain combining over a multi-channel
pool); HARQ-CC and HARQ-IR (hybrid retransmission with chase combining vs. incremental
redundancy); T URBO (extrinsic-information-exchange decoder); F OUNTAIN (rateless sampling with
confidence-based stopping); FEC-r (forward error correction at code rate r); ACM (the hand-coded
adaptive-coding-and-modulation router); D IVERSITY-SC-N and D IVERSITY-MRC-D ISCRETE -N
(wider-pool N -sample variants of the same selection / discrete-MRC operators).

     invested.
